Choice, Necessity, and the Reconciliation of Views
Critiques the choice/necessity distinction and offers reconciliation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the debate on whether the act occurs by choice or necessity and the two possibilities to reconcile the Mu'tazila and philosopher positions.

Core Idea
Choice vs necessity hinges on whether conditions produce inevitability.

This lesson delves into the critique of the simple choice/necessity distinction and presents two additional possibilities for how divine and human powers relate. It also reconciles the views of al-Qadi, the Mu'tazila, and the philosophers, concluding with the real difference between the schools.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the second part of the source text and its translation. This section begins with a critique of the simple distinction.
Notice the logical progression: critique, then two possibilities, then reconciliation.
2
Understand the critique
The passage argues that the absence of necessitation is only relative to the power itself; with all conditions present, necessity arises. But the Mu'tazila insist on choice even after conditions.
This is a key point of contention.
3
Examine the two possibilities
The first possibility concerns the nature of power and attachment; the second sees the servant's power as instrumental to God's power.
These are attempts to bridge the schools.
4
Follow the conclusion
The final part clarifies the real difference: God creates human power directly and by choice (Ash'ari) or by necessity (philosophers).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد السادس - المقصد الأول : في أفعاله تعالى وأفعال العباد معاً ثم اختلفوا (فقال الأستاذ بمجموع القدرتين على أن يتعلقا جميعاً بالفعل) نفسه، وجوز اجتماع المؤثرين على أثر واحد ( وقال القاضي : على أن تتعلق قدرة الله بأصل الفعل وقدرة العبد ) بصفته أعني بكونه طاعة ومعصية) إلى غير ذلك من الأوصاف التي لا توصف بها أفعاله تعالى ( كما في لطم اليتيم تأديباً أو إيذاء) فإن ذات اللطم واقعة بقدرة الله وتأثيره وكونه طاعة على الأول ومعصية على الثاني بقدرة بأن وقوع الفعل عند المعتزلة على سبيل الاختيار، وعند الحكماء على سبيل الوجوب لكنه غير تام لأن عدم الإيجاب، إنما هو بالنسبة إلى نفس القدرة. وأما مع تمام الشرائط من الإرادة وغيرها فليس إلا الوجوب، اللهم إلا أن يقال : مذهب المعتزلة أن صدور الفعل عن المختار ولو بعد تمام الشرائط على سبيل الصحة دون الوجوب بناء على كفاية الرجحان في الوقوع وإن كان مردوداً عند المحققين كما سبق، وهاهنا احتمالان آخران أحدهما أن يقال : ذات القدرة لا توجب الفعل عند المعتزلة إنما الإيجاب من التعلق الذي يجوز أن يكون بدله تعلق آخر وعند الحكيم ذات القدرة توجب التعلق المخصوص الموجب للفعل بأن تكون القدرة المؤثرة عندهم مع الفعل، كما أن القدرة الكاسبة عندنا كذلك والاحتمال الثاني أن يريد الحكماء بتعلق قدرة الله تعالى بقدرة العبد تعلقها بها من حيث تأثيرها في الفعل حتى تكون قدرة العبد كالآلة لقدرة الله تعالى وهي توجب الفعل بواسطتها، وعلى هذا يحصل الفرق بين مذهب الحكماء والمعتزلة ويندفع الاعتراض على القاضي بأنه لا معنى لنفي كون قدرة العبد متعلقة بقدرة الله تعالى لكنه خلاف ما اشتهر عن من إثبات القدرة المؤثرة للعبد بالنسبة إلى فعله الاختياري كما يفهم من الضابطة المذكورة أيضاً ويدل عليه تنصيص الآمدي فى أبكار الأفكار على أن فعل العبد عند إمام الحرمين واقع بقدرته مع تصريح المصنف بأنه موافق للحكماء في المذهب فالأظهر أن الفرق بين المذهبين إنما هو باعتبار أن خلق الله تعالى قدرة العبد بلا واسطة وبالاختيار وعند الفلاسفة بها الحكماء وبالإيجاب .
The Sixth Observatory - First Purpose: On His acts, may He be exalted, and the acts of servants together. Then they differed: al-Ustadh said that the two powers together both attach to the act itself, and he permitted the conjunction of two influences on one effect. Al-Qadi said that God's power attaches to the essence of the act and the servant's power to its quality, namely being obedience or disobedience, and other attributes that cannot be attributed to His acts, as in slapping the orphan as discipline or as injury: the essence of the slap occurs by God's power and His influence, and its being obedience in the first case and disobedience in the second case by the servant's power. [He said] that for the Mu'tazila the occurrence of the act is by way of choice, and for the philosophers by way of necessity, but this is not complete because the absence of necessitation is only relative to the power itself. But with the completion of conditions, such as will and others, there is nothing but necessity. Unless it is said: the Mu'tazila hold that the act proceeds from the free agent even after the completion of conditions as permissible without necessity, based on the sufficiency of preponderance for occurrence, though rejected by the verifiers as has preceded. Here there are two other possibilities: one is to say that the essence of power does not necessitate the act for the Mu'tazila; rather necessitation comes from the attachment which may be replaced by another attachment. For the philosopher, the essence of power necessitates the specific attachment that necessitates the act, since for them the influencing power is with the act, just as for us the acquisitive power is. The second possibility is that the philosophers intend by the attachment of God's power to the servant's power its attachment to it in terms of its influence in the act, so that the servant's power becomes like an instrument for God's power, and it necessitates the act through it. On this, the difference between the school of the philosophers and the Mu'tazila is obtained, and the objection against al-Qadi is repelled, that there is no meaning to denying that the servant's power is attached to God's power. But it contradicts what is known from him of affirming the servant's influencing power regarding his voluntary act, as is understood from the aforementioned rule. This is also indicated by al-Amidi's specification in Abkar al-Afkar that the servant's act, according to Imam al-Haramayn, occurs by his power, along with the author's statement that he agrees with the philosophers in doctrine. So the apparent view is that the difference between the two schools is only in that God's creation of the servant's power is without intermediary and by choice, whereas for the philosophers it is by necessity.
Source pages p. 164

Commentary
This passage presents the three main positions on how divine and human power relate to a single action. It begins by stating the topic: God's acts and human acts together. Then it reports three views: (1) al-Ustadh (Abu Ishaq al-Isfarayini) who holds that both powers together attach to the act itself, allowing two influences on one effect. (2) Al-Qadi (al-Baqillani) who holds that God's power attaches to the essence of the act and human power attaches to its quality (obedience/disobedience). (3) The philosophers (and, as will appear later, Imam al-Haramayn) who hold that the act occurs necessarily when conditions are met. The passage then expands on the distinction between Mu'tazila (choice) and philosophers (necessity). It notes that the difference is not absolute: for the Mu'tazila, after all conditions are complete, the act is still without necessity (only permissible), while for philosophers it becomes necessary. Two further possibilities are explored to reconcile the schools: first, that for Mu'tazila the power does not necessitate the act but rather the attachment can change; second, that the philosophers see the servant's power as instrumental to God's power. The passage concludes that the clearest difference is that God creates human power directly and by choice (Mu'tazila) or by necessity (philosophers). The author also notes a conflict in ascribing to al-Qadi a denial of the servant's power being attached to God's power, and refers to al-Amidi's testimony that Imam al-Haramayn actually agrees with the philosophers.

الوجوب
الرجحان
التعلق
القدرة المؤثرة
القدرة الكاسبة

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that the Mu'tazila and philosophers are completely opposite on choice vs. necessity, but the passage shows they can be reconciled.
Correction: The passage demonstrates that both can be seen as agreeing on the necessity after conditions, differing only on the nature of power creation.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the real difference between the Ash'ari school (as represented by al-Qadi/Imam al-Haramayn) and the philosophers?
A) Ash'aris deny human power, philosophers affirm it
B) Ash'aris say God creates human power directly and by choice, philosophers say by necessity
C) Ash'aris say the act occurs by necessity, philosophers by choice
D) Ash'aris believe in two powers, philosophers believe in one

Key Takeaway
The debate on human power ultimately reduces to whether God creates human power directly by choice or through necessity.

?

Objection and Answer: Does the Proof Apply to God?
Objection that proof applies to God and answer distinguishing created from eternal will.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Ash'ari response to the objection that the argument against independent agency applies equally to God by distinguishing between created will (al-irada al-muhdatha) and eternal will (al-irada al-qadima).

Core Idea
God's will is eternal and needs no preponderator; the servant's will is created and so must depend on another.

In this passage, al-Sanusi presents a powerful objection to the earlier proof: if the argument shows that human acts are not independent because they require a preponderator, then the same reasoning would seem to apply to God, undermining His freedom. The answer draws a crucial distinction between created and eternal will, showing why the regress stops at God. This lesson unpacks both the objection and the answer, clarifying a key nuance in Islamic theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the Objection
Read the first part of the passage where the objector argues that the same proof applies to God, negating His power and freedom.
Notice the phrase 'ينفي كون الله تعالى قادراً مختاراً'.
2
Understand the Answer's Distinction
Read the answer which distinguishes between created will (al-irada al-muhdatha) and eternal will (al-irada al-qadima).
The key is that created will requires a preponderator, but eternal will does not.
3
Trace the Regress Argument
See how the answer avoids infinite regress by stopping at God's eternal will.
The servant's will is created, so it depends on God's creation of a preponderator, but God's will is eternal, so no further preponderator is needed.
4
Reflect on the Implication
Consider why this distinction preserves God's autonomy while still explaining human dependence on God.
This is a central point in Ash'ari theology about divine causality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأورد عليه أن هذا ينفي كون الله تعالى قادراً مختاراً لإمكان إقامة الدلالة بعينها فيه ويقال: لو كان موجداً لفعله بالقدرة استقلالاً فلا بد أن يتمكن من فعله وتركه وأن يتوقف فعله على مرجح إلى آخر ما مر تقريره فالدليل منقوض بالواجب تعالى. وأجيب عن ذلك بالفرق بأن إرادة العبد محدثة أي الفعل يتوقف على مرجح هي الإرادة الجازمة لكن إرادة العبد محدثة فافتقرت إلى إرادة يخلقها الله فيه بلا إرادة واختيار منه دفعاً للتسلسل في الإرادة التي تفرض صدورها عنه وإرادة الله تعالى قديمة فلا تفتقر إلى إرادة أخرى.
And it was objected to this that this negates God Almighty being powerful and free, because it is possible to apply the very same proof to Him, and it is said: If He were the creator of His act by power independently, then He must be able to do it and leave it, and His act must depend on a preponderator, etc., as the previous elaboration, so the proof is refuted by the Necessary Being. And it was answered about that by the distinction that the will of the servant is created, i.e., the act depends on a preponderator which is the decisive will, but the will of the servant is created, so it needed a will that God creates in him without will and choice from him, to avoid infinite regress in the will that is supposed to proceed from him, and the will of God Almighty is eternal, so it does not need another will.
Source pages p. 168

Commentary
This passage presents an objection-and-answer dialectic. The objector argues that the third proof against the servant's independence applies equally to God: if God were the independent creator of His acts, then He too would need a preponderator, leading to the same conclusions, thus negating His freedom. The answer distinguishes between created and eternal will. The servant's act depends on a preponderator (the decisive will, al-irada al-jazima), but that will itself is created, so it requires a further preponderator created by God in the servant to avoid infinite regress. God's will, however, is eternal (qadima) and thus does not need another will to preponderate. This stops the regress at God. The answer maintains that the servant's acts are not independent because they ultimately trace back to God's creation of the will, while God's own acts are genuinely autonomous.

Key Terms
الإرادة المحدثة
الإرادة القديمة
التسلسل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The answer denies that God's will has any preponderator or reason.
Correction: The answer does not claim that God's will is without any preponderator; it claims His will is eternal and thus does not need an external preponderator. God's will is self-sufficient.

Checkpoint
Why does the servant's will require a further preponderator according to the answer?
A) Because it is eternal.
B) Because it is created.
C) Because it is free.
D) Because it is divine.

Key Takeaway
The distinction between created and eternal will is essential to preserve God's autonomy while explaining human dependence on divine causation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

How does this lesson’s distinction between your created will and God’s eternal will reshape your understanding of that moment? Will you now consciously trace your inability back to your createdness and trust God’s eternal plan more deeply?
?
The objector in this passage tried to measure God by human standards—assuming God’s will functions like ours. In what area of your life do you find yourself quietly applying human logic to God, perhaps expecting Him to ‘need’ a reason external to Himself? What would change if you fully lived that God’s will is eternal and needs no justification beyond itself?
?
This answer stops infinite regress by affirming that God directly creates the decisive will in you without your choice. Does that thought feel liberating or unsettling? How does it affect your sense of personal responsibility and your daily prayer? Are you ready to surrender even your ‘decisive will’ as a gift from Him, asking only that He guide it rightly?

The Refutation from Al-Lubab: The Dilemma of Divine Will
Al-Lubab refutes the answer by posing a dilemma about divine will.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical dilemma Al-Lubab poses against the distinction between eternal and created will, and why it undermines the previous answer.

Core Idea
The answer fails because it traps God between being a compeller or a necessary agent.

Al-Lubab critiques the answer that tried to save free will by distinguishing eternal will from created will. It shows that the answer still leads to a dilemma: either God is a compeller, or His act becomes necessary. This refutation forces us to reconsider whether the proof truly applies only to created beings.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage carefully, then its literal translation. Note the structure: it begins by naming the source and then presents a conditional argument.
The passage is incomplete in the source; we have enough to understand the dilemmic structure.
2
Identify the two horns of the dilemma
Look for the conditional 'if avoidance is impossible' and 'if it is possible'. Under each, trace the consequences that lead to God being either a compeller or the act becoming necessary.
The refutation uses a classic dilemmic form to show both options are problematic.
3
Analyze the criticism of the distinction
Notice the claim that the distinction between subject matters (created vs. eternal will) fails because the same logical proof structure applies to both. Ask yourself why this shows the proof is invalid.
This is the key move: a distinction in meaning doesn't save the proof if the reasoning is identical.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ورد في اللباب هذا الجواب الذي ذكر في الأربعين بأنه لا يدفع التقسيم المذكور إذ يقال: وإن لم يمكن الترك مع الإرادة القديمة كان موجباً لا قادراً مختاراً وإن أمكن فإن لم يتوقف فعله على مرجح اتفاقياً واقعاً بلا سبب واستغنى أيضاً الجائز عن المرجح وإن توقف عليه كان الفعل واجباً فيكون اضطرارياً والفرق الذي ذكرتموه في المدلول مع الاشتراك في الدليل دليل على بطلان الدليل وإنما يندفع النقض إذا بين عدم جريان الدليل في...
And it is reported in Al-Lubab that this answer mentioned in Al-Arba'in does not repel the mentioned division, for it is said: If avoidance is impossible with the eternal will, then He is a compeller, not powerful and free; and if it is possible, then if His act does not depend on a preponderator, it would be coincidental occurring without a cause, and the possible would also be independent of a preponderator; and if it depends, then the act would be necessary and thus compulsory. And the distinction you mentioned in the meaning with commonality in the proof is evidence of the proof's invalidity. And the refutation is only repelled if it is shown that the proof does not apply to...
Source pages p. 168

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the previous answer, cited from the work Al-Lubab (commenting on Al-Arba'in). It argues that the distinction between created and eternal will does not resolve the objection. The refutation poses a dilemma: If avoidance of the act is impossible with God's eternal will, then God is a compeller (mujib) rather than a free agent. If avoidance is possible, then we ask whether God's act depends on a preponderator or not. If it does not, then the act is coincidental and the principle that possibilities need a preponderator is abandoned. If it does depend on a preponderator, then the act becomes necessary (wajib) and compulsory (idtirari), just like the servant's act was claimed to be. Thus, the distinction fails because it shares the same logical structure (the proof) while differing only in the subject matter, which shows the proof is unsound. The refutation concludes that the objection is only repelled if it is shown that the proof does not apply to God at all, which the answer did not demonstrate.

Key Terms
اللباب
التقسيم
الموجب
الاضطرار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think Al-Lubab is claiming that God is actually a compeller.
Correction: Al-Lubab does not assert that God is a compeller; it shows that if one accepts the earlier answer's reasoning, the logical consequence is that God would be a compeller—which is unacceptable. It is a reduction ad absurdum.

Checkpoint
According to Al-Lubab, what follows if avoidance with God's eternal will is impossible?
A) God is free and powerful.
B) God is a compeller, not free.
C) The act is coincidental.
D) The proof is valid.

Key Takeaway
Al-Lubab shows that the attempted distinction collapses because it either makes God a compeller or makes His act necessary, thus failing to preserve free will.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Are you willing to admit that your reasoning might be flawed, even if it feels satisfying? This passage shows how one such attempt collapsed. What does that reveal about your own approach to theological questions?
?
If a logical dilemma about God cannot be resolved by your intellect, do you have the humility to accept that some truths are beyond human comprehension, or do you insist on forcing an answer? How does the failure of the answer in this passage challenge your intellectual pride and trust in your own reasoning?

Objection and Answer on Divine Will and Determinism
Objection-and-answer on divine determinant necessity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the objection that God's acts would be compelled by the same determinant argument is answered by distinguishing between originated and eternal will.

Core Idea
God's eternal will needs no external determinant, preserving His freedom.

In this passage, a key objection is raised against the Ash'ari argument that human acts require a determinant (murajjih). The objection claims the same reasoning would make God's acts compelled, violating His free agency. The answer draws a crucial distinction between created will (muhdatha) and eternal will (qadima) to resolve the dilemma.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
Read the objection: if human acts need a determinant, then the same logic applies to God, making His acts compelled too. Why would that be a problem?
The objection is that the argument would negate God's power and choice.
2
Examine the answer
The answer says human will is 'originated' (muhdatha) and thus depends on a chain of wills ending in God's creative will to avoid infinite regress. God's will is 'eternal' (qadima), so it does not require another determinant.
Notice the term 'دفعاً للتسلسل' (to avoid infinite regress).
3
Apply the distinction
Ask yourself: Why does eternal will not need a determinant? Because it is self-sufficient and uncaused. This preserves God's free agency while explaining human dependence.
This distinction is central to Ash'ari theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد السادس - المقصد الأول : في أفعاله تعالى وأفعال العباد
أن يوجد معه الفعل تارة ويعدم أخرى مع وجود ذلك المرجح فيهما فتخصيص أحد الوقتين بوجوده يحتاج إلى مرجح لما عرفت فلا يكون ما فرضناه مرجحاً تاماً هذا خلف، وإذا كان الفعل مع المرجح الذي ليس منه واجب الصدور عنه ( فيكون ) ذلك الفعل ( اضطرارياً ) لازماً لا اختيارياً بطريق الاستقلال كما زعموه ( وأورد عليه أن هذا ينفي كون الله تعالى قادراً ( مختاراً لإمكان إقامة الدلالة بعينها فيه ) ويقال : لو كان موجداً لفعله بالقدرة استقلالاً فلا بد أن يتمكن من فعله وتركه وأن يتوقف فعله على مرجح إلى آخر ما مر ،تقریره فالدليل منقوض بالواجب تعالى . ( وأجيب : ( عن ذلك ( بالفرق بأن إرادة العبد محدثة ) أي الفعل يتوقف على مرجح هي الإرادة الجازمة لكن إرادة العبد محدثة ( فافتقرت ) أن تنتهي ( إلى إرادة يخلقها الله فيه ) بلا إرادة واختيار منه ) ( دفعاً للتسلسل) في الإرادة التي تفرض صدورها عنه ( وإرادة الله تعالى قديمة فلا تفتقر إلى إرادة أخرى
The Sixth Observatory - First Objective: On His actions, Most High, and the actions of servants. That the act exists with it sometimes and is absent at other times while that determinant exists in both, so specifying one of the two times for its existence needs a determinant, as you have known, so what we assumed would not be a complete determinant; this is a contradiction. And if the act is with a determinant that is not from it, it is necessary of occurrence, so that act becomes compelled, necessary, not voluntary by way of independence as they claimed. And it was objected against this that this negates that God Most High is powerful, free, because it is possible to produce the same proof concerning Him. It is said: If He were the originator of His act by power independently, then He must be able to do it and leave it, and His act must depend on a determinant, etc., as previously established. So the proof is refuted by the Necessary of existence Most High. And it was answered: concerning that, by the difference that the will of the servant is originated, i.e., the act depends on a determinant which is the decisive will, but the will of the servant is originated, so it needs to end at a will that God creates in him, without will and choice from him, to avoid infinite regress in the will that is assumed to proceed from him; and the will of God Most High is eternal, so it does not need another will.
Source pages p. 168

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer pair in a kalam argument about determinism and free will. The objection claims that if human acts require a determinant (murajjih), then the same reasoning applies to God, making Him also under compulsion. The answer distinguishes human will (originated, finite) from divine will (eternal, self-sufficient). Human will depends on a series that ends in God's creative will to avoid infinite regress. God's will is eternal and requires no further determinant, thus God remains free. The commentator notes the distinction saves the argument from being applied to God.

Key Terms
مرجح
إرادة محدثة
إرادة قديمة
اضطرار
اختيار
تسلسل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that if God's will determines acts, then God Himself is subject to necessity.
Correction: This passage guards against that by clarifying that eternal will does not require an external determinant; it is self-sufficient.

Checkpoint
What is the key distinction made in the answer to the objection?
A) Human acts are compelled while God's acts are not.
B) The will of the servant is originated and finite, while God's will is eternal and needs no further determinant.
C) God's power is greater than human power.
D) The determinant for human acts is external compulsion.

Key Takeaway
The objection fails because human will is originated and finite, requiring a chain ending in God's eternal will, which needs no further determinant.

The Lubab Objection and the Ancient Determinant
Sanusi reports a counter-objection and responds by distinguishing the eternal determinant.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the response to the Lubab objection refines the concept of the ancient determinant to preserve divine independence.

Core Idea
The ancient determinant from God's essence needs no external cause, securing His free agency.

In this passage, Sanusi addresses a counter-objection from the work al-Lubab that challenges the previous answer regarding divine action and necessity. The objection claims that the answer does not resolve the tension between necessity and choice. Sanusi responds by clarifying that the eternal determinant (al-murajjih al-qadim) originates from God's essence and does not require another determinant, thus maintaining His independence. Let's examine this crucial distinction.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation once to get the flow of the argument. Note the dense dialectical style.
The passage includes a reported objection and a response.
2
Identify the dilemna from al-Lubab
Locate the objection that the previous answer fails because it leads to either compulsion or accident. Understand the two horns.
3
Study the response: the eternal determinant
The key move: the determinant in question must be originated, not eternal. The eternal determinant from God's essence needs no other cause.
This is the heart of the defense.
4
Contrast human and divine acts
Human acts depend on an external eternal determinant, making them compelled. Divine acts have the determinant from God Himself, preserving independence.

ورد في اللباب (هذا الجواب ) الذي ذكر في الأربعين (بأنه لا يدفع التقسيم المذكور) إذ يقال : وإن لم يمكن الترك مع الإرادة القديمة كان موجباً لا قادراً مختاراً وإن أمكن فإن لم يتوقف فعله على مرجح اتفاقياً واقعاً بلا سبب واستغنى أيضاً الجائز عن المرجح وإن توقف عليه كان الفعل واجباً فيكون اضطرارياً ) والفرق ) الذي ذكرتموه ( في المدلول مع الاشتراك في الدليل دليل على بطلان الدليل وإنما يندفع النقض إذا بين عدم جريان الدليل في
صورة التخلف ( وفيه ) أي في هذا الرد ( نظر فإن مآله ( أي مآل ما ذكر من الفرق بين إرادة العبد وإرادة الباري ) إلى تخصيص المرجح في قولنا ترجح فعله يحتاج إلى مرجح بالمرجح الحادث ) ، فإن المرجح القديم المتعلق إذ لا بالفعل الحادث في وقت لا يحتاج إلى مرجح آخر فيصير الاستدلال هكذا إن تمكن العبد من الفعل والترك، وتوقف الترجيح على مرجح وجب أن لا يكون ذلك المرجح منه وإلا كان حادثاً محتاجاً إلى مرجح آخر ولا يتسلسل بل ينتهي إلى مرجح قديم لا يكون العبد من ويجب الفعل معه فلا يكون العبد مستقلا فيه وأما فعل الباري فهو محتاج إلى مرجح قديم يتعلق في الأزل بالفعل الحادث في وقت معين وذلك المرجح القديم لا يحتاج إلى مرجح آخر فيكون تعالى مستقلاً في الفعل، وحينئذ لا يتجه النقض ( ويتم الجواب ) ، ولما كان القائل أن يقول : إذا وجب الفعل مع ذلك المرجح القديم موجباً لا مختاراً أشار إلى دفعه بقوله : ( وأما استلزام ذلك لوجوب الفعل منه فقد عرفت جوابه)، وهو أن الوجوب المترتب على الاختيار لا ينافيه بل يحققه، فإن قلت : نحن نقول : اختيار العبد أيضا يوجب فعله وهذا الوجوب لا ينافي كونه قادراً مختاراً، قلت : لا شك أن اختياره حادث وليس صادراً عنه باختياره وإلا نقلنا الكلام إلى ذلك الاختيار وتسلسل بل عن غيره فلا يكون هو مستقلاً في فعله باختياره بخلاف إرادة الباري تعالى فإنها مستندة إلى ذاته فوجوب الفعل بها لا ينافي في القدرة عليه لكن يتجه أن يقال : استناد إرادته القديمة إلى ذاته بطريق الإيجاب دون القدرة، فإذ وجب الفعل بما ليس اختياريا له تطرق إليه شائبة الإيجاب ) واعلم أن هذا

استقلاله
قوله : (بل ينتهي إلى مرجح (قديم فيه بحث أن المراد بالمرجح القديم هو الإرادة القديمة، كما دل عليه قوله فقد عرفت جوابه فلا نسلم السياق إذ قد تحققت باعترافه أن مبنى الكلام على وجوب الداعي في الفعل الاختياري .
قوله : (بل عن غيره فلا يكون هو إلخ فيه بحث وهو أن انتفاء صدور المرجح عن العبد باختياره لا يستدعي صدوره عن غيره لجواز أن يكون صادراً عنه من غير اختياره بلا لزوم التسلسل وقولهم : كل حادث مسبوق بالاختيار متفرع على أن لا مؤثر في الوجود إلا الله تعالى وإلا فالإحراق الحادث يستند إلى النار الحادث بالإيجاب، وذلك هاهنا أول المسألة، اللهم إلا أن يبنى الكلام على اعتراف الخصم بصدوره عن غيره فتأمل .
قوله : ( تطرق إليه شائبة الإيجاب ) يعني فلا فرق حينئذ فلا فرق حينئذ بين أفعال الله تعالى وأفعال العباد في الاضطرارية، نعم بينها فرق باعتبار أن الله تعالى مستقل في أفعاله لصدور المرجح عنه، والعبد ليس بمستقل في أفعاله لصدور المرجح عن غيره والحق أن الفعل وإن وجب بعد تعلق إرادته تعالى لكن لا يلزم الإيجاب لأن التعلق ليس بلازم لها بل هو مسبوق بتعلق آخر لا إلى نهاية كما
It was reported in al-Lubab that this answer, which was mentioned in al-Arba'in, is that it does not repel the mentioned division, for it is said: If leaving is not possible with the eternal will, then He is a necessitator, not powerful and free; and if it is possible, then if His act does not depend on a determinant, it happens by accident without cause, and the possible would also be independent of the determinant, and if it depends, the act is necessary, so it becomes compelled. And the difference you mentioned in the meaning, with the sharing in the proof, is evidence of the proof's invalidity. The objection is only repelled if it is shown that the proof does not apply in the case of non-occurrence. And in this response there is consideration, for its final import is to specify the determinant in our statement 'his act's preponderance needs a determinant' with the originated determinant. For the eternal determinant that relates to the originated act in a specific time does not need another determinant. So the argument becomes: if the servant is able to do and leave, and preponderance depends on a determinant, then it must be that this determinant is not from him, otherwise it would be originated and need another determinant, and no infinite regress occurs, but it ends at an eternal determinant that is not from the servant, and the act becomes necessary with it, so the servant is not independent in it. As for the act of the Creator, it needs an eternal determinant that relates in pre-eternity to the originated act in a specific time, and that eternal determinant does not need another determinant, so He Most High is independent in the act, and then the objection is not valid, and the answer is complete. And because the speaker might say: 'If the act is necessary with that eternal determinant, then He is a necessitator, not free,' he indicated to refute it by his saying: 'As for the implication of that for the necessity of the act from Him, you have already known its answer,' which is that the necessity that follows choice does not contradict it but rather confirms it. For if you say: 'We say: the servant's choice also necessitates his act, and this necessity does not contradict his being powerful and free,' I say: 'There is no doubt that his choice is originated and does not proceed from him by his choice, otherwise we would transfer the discourse to that choice and it would regress infinitely, but rather it is from another, so he is not independent in his act by his choice, unlike the will of the Creator Most High, for it is based on His essence, so the necessity of the act by it does not contradict His power over it. However, it can be said: the dependence of His eternal will on His essence is by way of necessitation, not by power, so if the act is necessary by what is not volitional for Him, the taint of necessitation enters into it.' And know that this... his independence... His saying: 'But it ends at an eternal determinant' contains a discussion that the meaning of the eternal determinant is the eternal will, as indicated by his saying 'you have already known its answer,' so we do not accept the context, for you have verified by his admission that the basis of the discourse is on the necessity of the motive in voluntary action. His saying: 'But from another, so he is not independent, etc.' contains a discussion: that the non-occurrence of the determinant from the servant by his choice does not necessitate its occurrence from another, for it is possible that it occurs from him without his choice without necessitating infinite regress. And their statement: 'Every originated thing is preceded by choice' is based on the assumption that no effect exists except by God Most High; otherwise, originated burning is attributed to fire originated by necessitation, and that is the beginning of the issue here. Unless the discourse is built on the opponent's admission of its occurrence from another, so ponder. His saying: 'the taint of necessitation enters into it' means then there is no difference, then there is no difference between the actions of God Most High and the actions of servants in compulsion. Yes, there is a difference between them insofar as God Most High is independent in His actions because the determinant proceeds from Him, while the servant is not independent in his actions because the determinant proceeds from another. The truth is that the act, even if it becomes necessary after the attachment of His will, does not entail necessitation, because the attachment is not necessary for it, but rather it is preceded by another attachment without end, as...
Source pages p. 168

Commentary
This passage continues the debate. It first reports a counter-objection from the work al-Lubab: the previous answer does not resolve the problem because if the eternal will makes the act necessary, God becomes a necessitator; if it does not, the act could be accidental. The response clarifies that the ancient determinant (al-murajjih al-qadim) relates to the originated act in a specific time and does not need another determinant. This resolves the objection: human acts end at an external eternal determinant, making them compelled, while divine acts have that determinant from God's essence, preserving independence. The commentator then addresses a further nuance: the necessity that follows choice does not negate free will; however, if the divine will depends on essence by necessitation, a taint of compulsion might appear. Marginal notes discuss the meaning of 'ancient determinant' and the nature of choice. The conclusion affirms that God is independent because the determinant originates from Him, unlike servants. The truth is that the act's necessity after will does not mean compulsion, as the attachment is not necessary.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the eternal will makes God passive or compelled, like a cause-effect mechanism.
Correction: The eternal will from God's essence is not an external cause; it is self-sufficient and the basis of free choice, not compulsion.

Checkpoint
According to the response to the Lubab objection, why does the eternal determinant not lead to compulsion for God?
A) Because God can do contrary acts without a determinant.
B) Because the eternal determinant is from God's essence and does not need another determinant, preserving His independence.
C) Because the servant's will is also eternal.
D) Because the act occurs by accident, not necessity.

Key Takeaway
The eternal determinant from God's essence secures His independence, unlike human acts which depend on external determinants.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about your own good deeds, do you secretly believe they are forced by God's decree, or do you truly rely on His eternal will that empowers you? How would your daily actions change if you deeply felt that God's eternal determinant is the source of your independence, not your own strength?
?
Consider a moment you felt your faith was accidental or without a solid foundation. How does the truth that God's will is not compelled by anything outside Himself challenge you to take your relationship with Him more seriously?
?
If God's act of creation is independent and free, what does that mean for your trust in His promises? Are you still content to obey Him when His commands seem difficult, knowing that He is not influenced by your performance?

Divine Attributes
advanced
8 min
Necessity from Choice and Divine Independence
Sanusi defends the necessity from choice and distinguishes divine from human agency.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how necessity arising from choice confirms free will rather than negating it, and the final distinction between divine and human agency.

Core Idea
Necessity from self-sufficient choice is the mark of true freedom, not compulsion.

This passage continues the debate by addressing a further objection: if the act becomes necessary with the eternal determinant, does that not make God a necessitator? The response argues that necessity based on choice confirms free will. It then contrasts this with human agency, where choice depends on external factors. Finally, it raises a subtle point about the divine will's relation to essence, questioning whether a taint of necessitation might appear. Let's trace this nuanced argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation to grasp the flow of objection and response.
The passage ends abruptly; we have only the beginning of a concluding thought.
2
Understand the objection and its refutation
The objector says: if the act is necessary with the eternal determinant, God is a necessitator. The author responds that necessity from choice confirms free will, not negates it.
Key phrase: 'al-wujub al-mutaratib 'ala al-ikhtiyar'.
3
Compare with human choice
Human choice is originated and not self-caused; it leads to infinite regress unless it comes from an external source, making the human not independent.
4
Analyze the final nuance
The divine will is based on essence, but if that relation is by necessitation (ijab) rather than power (qudra), a taint of compulsion might be implied. The passage leaves this open.
This is a deep point in kalām.

Misconception & Correction

According to the passage, why does the necessity of the act from divine will not contradict free will?
A) Because God can change His will at any time.
B) Because divine will is based on His essence, not an external cause, and the necessity follows choice.

?

The Objection About the Preponderant and Infinite Regress
Al-Ījī presents an objection and inference about the preponderant.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the servant cannot be the source of his own preponderant and how this differs from God's independence.

Core Idea
The servant's action requires a preponderant that is not from him, making him dependent.

This lesson examines a key objection in the debate about human free will and divine agency. Al-Ījī presents a logical argument that the preponderant for human action must be created and thus not from the human, leading to divine determination. Understanding this argument is crucial for navigating the Ashʿarī position on acquired action (kasb).

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the compressed style and the logical connectors.
2
Identify the structure of the argument
The passage begins with an objection that reduces the previous difference to specifying the preponderant as created. Then it presents an inference about the servant's action requiring a preponderant not from him to avoid regress. Finally, it contrasts with God's case where the eternal preponderant does not regress.
Look for the shift from 'the servant' to 'the Creator'.
3
Contrast the cases of servant and God
Note how the servant's preponderant must be created and leads to a regress if it were from him, whereas God's preponderant is eternal and does not need another preponderant.
This contrast is the heart of the argument for divine omnipotence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
صورة التخلف ( وفيه ) أي في هذا الرد ( نظر فإن مآله ( أي مآل ما ذكر من الفرق بين إرادة العبد وإرادة الباري ) إلى تخصيص المرجح في قولنا ترجح فعله يحتاج إلى مرجح بالمرجح الحادث ) ، فإن المرجح القديم المتعلق به لا بالفعل الحادث في وقت لا يحتاج إلى مرجح آخر فيصير الاستدلال هكذا إن تمكن العبد من الفعل والترك، وتوقف الترجيح على مرجح وجب أن لا يكون ذلك المرجح منه وإلا كان حادثاً محتاجاً إلى مرجح آخر ولا يتسلسل بل ينتهي إلى مرجح قديم لا يكون العبد منه ويجب الفعل معه فلا يكون العبد مستقلا فيه وأما فعل الباري فهو محتاج إلى مرجح قديم يتعلق في الأزل بالفعل الحادث في وقت معين وذلك المرجح القديم لا يحتاج إلى مرجح آخر فيكون تعالى مستقلاً في الفعل، وحينئذ لا يتجه النقض ( ويتم الجواب ) ، ولما كان القائل أن يقول : إذا وجب الفعل مع ذلك المرجح القديم موجباً لا مختاراً أشار إلى دفعه بقوله : ( وأما استلزام ذلك لوجوب الفعل منه فقد عرفت جوابه)، وهو أن الوجوب المترتب على الاختيار لا ينافيه بل يحققه، فإن قلت : نحن نقول : اختيار العبد أيضا يوجب فعله وهذا الوجوب لا ينافي كونه قادراً مختاراً، قلت : لا شك أن اختياره حادث وليس صادراً عنه باختياره وإلا نقلنا الكلام إلى ذلك الاختيار وتسلسل بل عن غيره فلا يكون هو مستقلاً في فعله باختياره بخلاف إرادة الباري تعالى فإنها مستندة إلى ذاته فوجوب الفعل بها لا ينافي في القدرة عليه لكن يتجه أن يقال : استناد إرادته القديمة إلى ذاته بطريق الإيجاب دون القدرة، فإذ وجب الفعل بما ليس اختياريا له تطرق إليه شائبة الإيجاب )
The form of non-occurrence: (and in it) i.e. in this refutation (there is a consideration, for its final outcome) i.e. the final outcome of what was mentioned of the difference between the will of the servant and the will of the Creator (is to specify the preponderant in our statement 'the preponderance of his action needs a preponderant' to the created preponderant). For the eternal preponderant that is related to the created act in a time does not need another preponderant. So the inference becomes like this: if the servant is capable of action and omission, and the preponderance depends on a preponderant, then it must be that that preponderant is not from him; otherwise it would be created, needing another preponderant, and there would be no infinite regress, but it ends at an eternal preponderant which the servant is not from, and the act becomes necessary with it, so the servant is not independent in it. As for the action of the Creator, it needs an eternal preponderant that relates in eternity to the created act at a specific time, and that eternal preponderant does not need another preponderant, so He, exalted, is independent in the action. Then the refutation is not directed. (And the answer is completed). And since the objector might say: if the act becomes necessary with that eternal preponderant, then He would be a necessitator, not a free agent, he indicated its refutation by his saying: 'As for the implication of that for the necessity of the act from Him, you have known its answer,' which is that the necessity resulting from choice does not contradict it but rather realizes it. For if you say: we say that the servant's choice also necessitates his action, and this necessity does not contradict his being powerful and free, I say: there is no doubt that his choice is created and not proceeding from him by his choice; otherwise we would transfer the discourse to that choice and infinite regress, but rather from another, so he is not independent in his action by his choice, unlike the will of the Creator, exalted, for it is based on His essence, so the necessity of the act by it does not contradict power over it. However, it may be said: the dependence of His eternal will on His essence is by way of necessitation without power, so if the act becomes necessary by what is not voluntary for Him, the tinge of necessity attaches to it.
Source pages p. 169

Commentary
This is an objection and answer about the nature of the preponderant in free actions. The passage begins by stating that the objection reduces the argument to specifying the preponderant as a created one. It then explains that the eternal preponderant (the divine will) does not require another preponderant, so the servant's preponderant cannot be from him to avoid infinite regress. In contrast, God's action depends on an eternal preponderant that relates to the act from eternity, making God independent. The objector then claims that this leads to necessity and compulsion in God. The answer clarifies that necessity from choice does not contradict freedom but confirms it. Then a further objection arises: why doesn't the servant's choice also necessitate without contradicting freedom? The answer explains that the servant's choice is created and not itself chosen, leading to a regress if it were chosen; thus the servant is not independent. God's will, on the other hand, is based on His essence, so the necessity it brings does not negate power. However, a final nuance is that God's will is necessitated from His essence without power, which could imply a tinge of compulsion. Thus the passage deals with the distinction between divine and human agency.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The servant's preponderant must be from him to preserve free will.
Correction: If the preponderant were from the servant, it would be created and require another preponderant, leading to infinite regress. Thus the preponderant must be not from him.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why does the servant's preponderant need to be not from him?
A) To avoid infinite regress
B) To ensure the servant is independent
C) To make the act necessary
D) To allow the eternal preponderant to act

Key Takeaway
The servant cannot be the source of his own preponderant because that would entail infinite regress; therefore, the preponderant must come from an external eternal source, making the servant dependent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In every choice you make today—from the trivial to the significant—do you honestly recognize that the decisive factor (preponderant) for that action comes not from you, but from the eternal will of Allah? How does that realization change the way you approach your decisions right now?
?
The passage argues that if the preponderant were from you, it would lead to an infinite regress and futility. Where in your life have you been trying to be the ultimate source of your own actions, feeling exhausted or anxious? What would it look like to genuinely surrender that role to Allah today?
?
You claim to believe that Allah is independent and you are dependent. Yet, when you commit a sin or succeed in obedience, do you internally credit yourself or feel self-sufficient? Examine your heart: are there actions where you unconsciously assume the preponderant is from you, forgetting that even your capacity to choose is sustained by an eternal preponderant?

Necessity from Choice and the Distinction Between Wills
Al-Ījī answers an objection about necessity and distinguishes divine and human wills.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how necessity arising from choice is compatible with free agency and why the servant's will differs from God's.

Core Idea
Necessity from choice does not negate freedom, but the servant's choice is not itself chosen, unlike God's will.

This lesson continues the discussion on the preponderant, addressing a further objection that God's action would be necessary and thus compulsory. The answer clarifies that necessity from choice is not contradictory to freedom. A final nuance distinguishes human will, which is created and dependent, from divine will, which is inherent and based on essence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Pay attention to the objection about necessity and the answer that necessity from choice is compatible.
Notice the dialectical structure: objection then refutation.
2
Understand the answer about necessity from choice
The author argues that necessity resulting from choice does not contradict free agency but rather realizes it. This is a key Ashʿarī response to the charge of compulsion.
Contrast with the objector's assumption that necessity always implies compulsion.
3
Grasp the distinction between divine and human will
The passage explains that the servant's choice is created and not itself chosen, leading to dependence, while God's will is based on His essence, making Him independent. However, a final nuance suggests that God's will is necessitated from His essence, which may imply a tinge of compulsion.
This nuance is a point of further debate; the author presents it as a potential issue.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Necessity of action always implies compulsion and negates free will.
Correction: The passage explains that necessity resulting from choice does not contradict freedom but rather realizes it. It is the origin of the necessity that matters, not the necessity itself.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is the servant not independent in his action by his choice?
A) Because his choice is created and not proceeding from him by his choice
B) Because his choice is eternal
C) Because he lacks power
D) Because his choice is necessitated by God

Key Takeaway
Necessity from choice is compatible with free agency, but the servant's will is created and dependent, while God's will is based on His essence, though it raises a nuance of necessitation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about your own daily choices—like deciding to pray or to speak a kind word—do you ever feel a tension between your sense of freedom and the awareness that your choice is created by God? How does this lesson's claim that your choice is 'not chosen' but 'from another' affect the way you see your own responsibility for those actions? This week, when you make a decision, pause to acknowledge that both the choice and its outcome are from God. How does that change your sense of dependence?
?
If God's will is necessitated from His essence, and that brings a 'tinge of compulsion' to His actions, how does that affect your trust in His acts? Does it make you uneasy, or does it reassure you that God's actions are perfectly consistent with His nature? Examine your heart: do you truly believe that whatever God does—whether in your life or in the world—is good, even if it feels necessary? Commit to reciting 'Hasbun Allah wa ni'm al-wakil' the next time you face a situation that seems unavoidable.
?
The lesson ends with a nuance: God's will is not voluntary in the way we might think. How does this challenge your mental image of God as 'absolutely free'? Can you accept that God's freedom is not like human freedom, and that His actions flow from His essence without diminishing His perfection? If you have ever doubted God's justice or wisdom in a difficult trial, could it be because you were measuring His will by human standards? Repent from imposing your limited understanding on His infinite nature, and ask Him to increase your certainty.

Counter-Objection: Divine Foreknowledge Necessitates Acts
The author argues that divine foreknowledge poses the same necessitation problem as created acts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why divine foreknowledge poses the same problem for Mu'tazilites as divine creation does for Ash'arites, and how a response based on the directionality of knowledge is answered.

Core Idea
Divine foreknowledge necessitates acts just as creation does, undermining Mu'tazilite arguments.

The Ash'arite counter-objection turns the Mu'tazilite argument on its head. If the Mu'tazilites claim that divine creation of acts invalidates human freedom, then divine foreknowledge must do the same. This paragraph presents that challenge and a powerful defense by al-Razi, along with an objection and its reply.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the argument's flow: claim, first aspect, al-Razi's bold statement, objection, and response.
The passage contains a gloss at the end; focus on the main argument.
2
Identify the first aspect
The first aspect states that if God knows an act will not occur, its non-occurrence becomes necessary for the servant. Understand why this invalidates choice and obligation.
This is the core parallel: foreknowledge leads to necessity just as creation does.
3
Analyze al-Razi's claim
Al-Razi says no one can refute this except by adopting Hisham's view (that God does not know future contingents). Consider the implication: the argument forces a radical choice.
This shows the strength of the counter-objection in the Ash'arite view.
4
Understand the objection and response
The objection says knowledge follows the known, so foreknowledge does not cause the act. The response argues that the necessity runs from knowledge to act, like cause to effect, not vice versa.
This resolves the apparent contradiction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
( ثم إن هذا الذي ذكروه ( إن لزم القائل بعدم استقلاله العبد في أفعاله ) فهو لازم لهم أيضاً لوجوه : الأول إن ما علم الله عدمه) من أفعاله ( فهو واجب العبد ) وإلا جاز ذلك الانقلاب ) ولا مخرج عنهما ) لفعل العبد، ( وأنه يبطل الاختيار) إذ لا قدرة على الواجب والممتنع فيبطل حينئذ التكليف وأخواته لابتنائها على القدرة والاختيار بالاستقلال كما ذكرتم فما لزمنا في مسألة خلق الأفعال، فقد لزمكم في مسألة علم الله تعالى بالأشياء، قال الإمام الرازي : ولو اجتمع جملة العقلاء لم يقدروا على أن يوردوا على هذا الوجه حرفاً إلا بالتزام مذهب هشام، أنه تعالى لا يعلم الأشياء قبل وقوعها واعترض عليه بأن العلم تابع للمعلوم على معنى أنهما يتطابقان والأصل في هذه المطابقة هو المعلوم ألا یری أن صورة الفرس مثلا على الجدار، إنما كانت على هذه الهيئة المخصوصة لأن الفرس في حد نفسه هكذا ولا يتصور أن ينعكس الحال بينهما، فالعلم بأن زيداً سيقوم غداً مثلاً إنما هو الصدور عن قوله : ( واعترض عليه بأن العلم تابع إلخ) قد يجاب عنه بأن المراد من الوجه الأول أن الله تعالى إذ علم في الأزل أن العبد يختار فيما لا يزال فعلاً معيناً فيتصف به لم يكن بد من وقوع هذا الاختيار والاتصاف المتفرع عليه فيما لا يزال وإلا لانقلب العلم جهلا، فتعلق علمه تعالى بوجود شيء يستلزم وجوده بنحو من استلزام السبب للسبب لا عكسه حتى يرد الاعتراض.
(Then verily this that they mentioned – if it is necessary for the one who says that the servant is not independent in his acts – then it is also necessary for them, by aspects: The first: that what God knows would not occur of his acts, it is necessary for the servant not to do it, otherwise change would be possible, and there is no way out of both for the servant's act, and that invalidates choice, because there is no power over the necessary and the impossible, so then obligation and its like would be invalid because they are based on power and choice by independence as you mentioned. So what is necessary for us in the issue of the creation of acts, is necessary for you in the issue of God's knowledge of things. Imam al-Razi said: Even if a whole group of rational people gathered, they could not present a single letter against this aspect except by adopting the position of Hisham, that He, may He be exalted, does not know things before they occur. And it was objected to him that knowledge follows the known, in the sense that they correspond, and the origin in this correspondence is the known. Do you not see that the image of a horse on the wall, for example, is only in this specific form because the horse in itself is thus, and it is not possible for the situation to be reversed between them. So knowledge that Zayd will stand tomorrow, for example, is only the issuing from... (He said: 'And it was objected to him that knowledge follows, etc.') It may be answered: That the meaning of the first aspect is that God, when He knew from eternity that the servant will choose in the future a specific act and will be described by it, it was inevitable that this choice and the description branching from it occur in the future, otherwise knowledge would turn into ignorance. So the attachment of His knowledge, may He be exalted, to the existence of a thing necessitates its existence in a way similar to the necessitation of the cause for the effect, not vice versa, so that the objection is countered.
Source pages p. 173

Commentary
This passage is a counter-objection by the Ash'arite against the Mu'tazilite proof. The author argues that the very same problem that the Mu'tazilites claimed invalidates obligation without independence actually applies to them as well, due to divine foreknowledge. The first aspect: if God knows that a particular act will not occur, then its non-occurrence becomes necessary for the servant; otherwise, God's knowledge would change (impossible). This leaves no power over the necessary or impossible, thus invalidating choice and consequently obligation. So the Ash'arite says: what you claim necessitates us (creation of acts) also necessitates you (foreknowledge). Imam al-Razi states that no one can refute this argument except by adopting Hisham's position that God does not know things before they occur. An objection is then raised: knowledge follows the known, so it is the known that determines knowledge, not the reverse; thus, foreknowledge does not cause the act. The gloss provides a response: the meaning of the first aspect is that God's eternal knowledge of the servant's future choice necessitates that very choice, because if it were otherwise, the knowledge would be ignorance. The relation is one of necessary implication from the cause (divine knowledge) to the effect (the act), not the opposite, so the objection fails.

Key Terms
علم الله
وجوب الفعل
اختيار
مذهب هشام
تبعية العلم للمعلوم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think divine foreknowledge is compatible with human free will without any necessitation.
Correction: The argument shows that divine foreknowledge logically necessitates the known act, just as direct creation would, thus posing the same challenge to human freedom.

Checkpoint
What is the first aspect of the counter-objection against the Mu'tazilites?
A) If God knows an act will not occur, it becomes impossible for the servant, invalidating choice.
B) God's knowledge is derived from the known, so it does not necessitate.
C) The servant creates his own knowledge.
D) Obligation is based on power, not choice.

Key Takeaway
Divine foreknowledge necessitates human acts in the same way as divine creation, challenging the Mu'tazilite position on human freedom.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider that God's eternal knowledge of your every action necessitates that action, does it shake your sense of genuine choice? How do you reconcile your daily decisions, like what to eat or how to pray, with the reality that God already knew them? Commit to one specific area today where you will consciously choose to obey Allah, not as a passive actor but as one who actively exercises faith despite the theological paradox.
?
Have you ever blamed your sins on divine foreknowledge, thinking, 'God knew I would do this, so it was inevitable'? How does the Ash'arite response—that divine knowledge necessitates as a cause, not as a passive reflection—challenge you to take full responsibility for your actions? Write down one sin you have committed and, without excusing it, ask Allah for forgiveness while committing to avoid it again.
?
The passage argues that if God knows something will not happen, it becomes impossible for you to do it. Is there a good deed you have been putting off, assuming you have all the time in the world? How does the reality that God's knowledge makes some futures inevitable and others impossible change your sense of urgency? Identify one act of worship you can perform within the next hour, and do it now.

The First Objection: Divine Foreknowledge Necessitates Acts
First objection and al-Rāzī's endorsement.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first objection against the Ashʿarite denial of human independence: that divine foreknowledge makes acts impossible, thereby negating human choice and moral obligation.

Core Idea
Divine foreknowledge, if it determines the act, destroys human freedom.

This lesson presents the first of four arguments against the Ashʿarite position that humans are not independent in their actions. The objection draws an analogy between the creation of actions (which the Ashʿarites deny humans cause) and divine foreknowledge: if God’s knowledge makes an act inevitable, then humans lose power and moral responsibility. The prominent theologian al-Rāzī is cited to emphasize the strength of this objection.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection passage
Carefully read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the structure: it first states the objection, then uses an analogy with the creation of actions, and ends with al-Rāzī's endorsement.
2
Identify the core claim
The objection claims that if God knows an act will not occur, that act becomes impossible for the servant. This negates power and choice, thus invalidating religious obligation and related concepts.
The key terms are 'wājib' (necessary) and 'mumtaniʿ' (impossible).
3
Reflect on al-Rāzī's statement
Al-Rāzī asserts that this objection is unanswerable unless one adopts Hishām b. al-Ḥakam's view that God does not know particulars before they occur. Consider why this is so strong.
Hishām's view is considered extreme and contrary to mainstream Islamic theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم إن هذا الذي ذكروه ( إن لزم القائل بعدم استقلاله العبد في أفعاله ) فهو لازم لهم أيضاً لوجوه : الأول إن ما علم الله عدمه) من أفعاله ( فهو واجب العبد ) وإلا جاز ذلك الانقلاب ) ولا مخرج عنهما ) لفعل العبد، ( وأنه يبطل الاختيار) إذ لا قدرة على الواجب والممتنع فيبطل حينئذ التكليف وأخواته لابتنائها على القدرة والاختيار بالاستقلال كما ذكرتم فما لزمنا في مسألة خلق الأفعال، فقد لزمكم في مسألة علم الله تعالى بالأشياء، قال الإمام الرازي : ولو اجتمع جملة العقلاء لم يقدروا على أن يوردوا على هذا الوجه حرفاً إلا بالتزام مذهب هشام، أنه تعالى لا يعلم الأشياء قبل وقوعها واعترض عليه بأن العلم تابع للمعلوم على معنى أنهما يتطابقان والأصل في هذه المطابقة هو المعلوم ألا یری أن صورة الفرس مثلا على الجدار، إنما كانت على هذه الهيئة المخصوصة لأن الفرس في حد نفسه هكذا ولا يتصور أن ينعكس الحال بينهما، فالعلم بأن زيداً سيقوم غداً مثلاً إنما الصدور عن وهو ما نحن نمنعه، وأما وجوب ثواب المطيع بمقتضى الوعد فلا ننكره كيف وقد قال عز من قائل يبدل القول لدي ﴾ [ق: ۲۹] فإن قلت : دوام الترتب أو أكثريته شرط في العاديات كما مر ، فكيف يكون عقاب العاصي قلت : الأكثر بالنسبة إلى عامة المكلفين هو عقاب العاصي . قوله : (واعترض عليه بأن العلم تابع إلخ) قد يجاب عنه بأن المراد من الوجه الأول أن الله تعالى إذ علم في الأزل أن العبد يختار فيما لا يزال فعلاً معيناً فيتصف به لم يكن بد من وقوع هذا الاختيار والاتصاف المتفرع عليه فيما لا يزال وإلا لانقلب العلم جهلا، فتعلق علمه تعالى بوجود شيء يستلزم وجوده بنحو من استلزام السبب للسبب لا عكسه حتى يرد الاعتراض. يتحقق إذا كان هو في نفسه بحيث يقوم فيه دون العكس فلا مدخل للعلم في وجوب الفعل وامتناعه وسلب القدرة والاختيار وإلا لزم أن لا يكون تعالى فعلاً مختاراً لكونه عالما بأفعاله وجوداً وعدماً .
Then indeed what they mentioned (if it is necessary for the one who denies the servant's independence in his actions) then it is necessary for them as well, for several aspects: The first: that what God knows will not exist from the servant's actions is necessary for the servant (otherwise that inversion would be possible) and there is no escape from them for the servant's action, and (that it nullifies choice) because there is no power over the necessary and impossible, so then obligation and its likes are invalidated because they are based on power and choice independently as you mentioned, so what is necessary for us in the matter of the creation of actions is necessary for you in the matter of God's knowledge of things. Imam al-Razi said: If all the rational people gathered, they could not bring a single letter against this aspect except by adopting the doctrine of Hisham, that God does not know things before their occurrence. And an objection was raised against it: that knowledge follows the known in the sense that they correspond, and the origin in this correspondence is the known. Do you not see that the image of a horse on the wall, for example, is only in this specific form because the horse in itself is thus, and it is inconceivable that the situation be reversed. So the knowledge that Zaid will stand tomorrow, for example, is only derived from—and that is what we deny. As for the obligation of reward for the obedient by virtue of the promise, we do not deny it—how could we, while Allah Almighty said: 'The word is changed with Me' (Q 50:29)? If you say: Continuity of correlation or its frequency is a condition in habitual matters as previously mentioned, so how is the punishment of the disobedient? I say: The majority, with respect to the generality of the obligated, is the punishment of the disobedient. His saying: (And an objection was raised against it that knowledge follows etc.) It may be answered that what is meant by the first aspect is that God, when He knew in eternity that the servant will choose a particular action in the future and be described by it, then the occurrence of this choice and the derived description in the future is inevitable, otherwise knowledge would turn into ignorance. So His knowledge of the existence of something necessitates its existence in the manner of the cause necessitating the effect, not the reverse, such that the objection would apply. It is realized when it is in itself such that it stands without the reverse, so there is no role for knowledge in the necessity or impossibility of the act and the removal of power and choice; otherwise it would necessitate that God is not a free agent, since He knows His actions, existent and non-existent.
Source pages p. 173

Commentary
This passage presents the first of four objections (wujūh) that the author argues are binding on the Ashʿarite position of denying human independence. The objection is that if God knows from eternity that a servant will not perform an act, that act becomes impossible for the servant (wājib al-ʿabd), negating his power and thus invalidating moral obligation. Al-Rāzī is cited to emphasize the strength of this objection: only by adopting Hishām b. al-Ḥakam’s view that God does not know particulars before they occur can one avoid it. An objection is then raised: knowledge follows the known, so knowledge does not cause the act. The response distinguishes that divine knowledge, unlike human knowledge, necessitates the known in the manner of a cause. The passage concludes by warning that if knowledge did not necessitate, God would not be a free agent (fāʿil mukhtār).

Key Terms
علم
واجب
ممتنع
اختيار
استقلال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Divine foreknowledge does not affect human freedom at all.
Correction: The objection argues that if God knows an act will not occur, it becomes impossible for the human to perform it, directly negating power and choice.

Checkpoint
According to the first objection, what does God's knowledge that a servant will not do an act entail?
A) The act remains possible
B) The act becomes impossible for the servant
C) The servant gains power
D) The act becomes obligatory for the servant

Key Takeaway
The first objection forces the Ashʿarite to either admit that divine foreknowledge negates human choice or adopt the extreme position that God does not know future particulars.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider that Allah already knows every choice you will ever make, does that make you feel trapped by fate or liberated by His mercy? How does that feeling shape your trust in Him today?
?
If you truly believed that your actions are already known and thereby fixed, would you still pray for guidance and strength? What does your answer reveal about your real view of divine foreknowledge?
?
Al-Rāzī says only denying God's knowledge of particulars escapes this logic. Would you rather deny God's omniscience than admit your own powerlessness? What does that say about your reliance on your own ability versus Allah's grace?

Counter-Objection: Knowledge Follows the Known
Counter-objection and its refutation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the counter-objection that knowledge follows the known and does not cause it, and the Ashʿarite response that divine knowledge necessitates the known as a cause.

Core Idea
Divine knowledge, unlike human knowledge, necessitates its object in the manner of a cause.

This lesson continues the discussion of the first objection. After al-Rāzī's strong endorsement, an objection is raised: knowledge typically follows the known and does not cause it, so how can divine foreknowledge make acts impossible? The Ashʿarite response clarifies that divine knowledge is different—it actively necessitates the known, like a cause.

Guided Path
1
Read the counter-objection
Read the passage starting with 'واعترض عليه...'. Understand the objection: knowledge corresponds to the known, so the known is prior; thus knowledge does not cause the act.
2
Understand the response
The response argues that divine knowledge necessitates the known in the manner of a cause (istilzām al-sabab li-l-sabab). It is not passive but active. If the objector's view held, even God would not be a free agent.
The key distinction is between human knowledge (passive) and divine knowledge (active).
3
Reflect on the implication
Consider the final point: if divine knowledge did not necessitate, then God's own actions would not be determined by His knowledge, implying He is not a free agent (fāʿil mukhtār). This is unacceptable.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم إن هذا الذي ذكروه ( إن لزم القائل بعدم استقلاله العبد في أفعاله ) فهو لازم لهم أيضاً لوجوه : الأول إن ما علم الله عدمه) من أفعاله ( فهو واجب العبد ) وإلا جاز ذلك الانقلاب ) ولا مخرج عنهما ) لفعل العبد، ( وأنه يبطل الاختيار) إذ لا قدرة على الواجب والممتنع فيبطل حينئذ التكليف وأخواته لابتنائها على القدرة والاختيار بالاستقلال كما ذكرتم فما لزمنا في مسألة خلق الأفعال، فقد لزمكم في مسألة علم الله تعالى بالأشياء، قال الإمام الرازي : ولو اجتمع جملة العقلاء لم يقدروا على أن يوردوا على هذا الوجه حرفاً إلا بالتزام مذهب هشام، أنه تعالى لا يعلم الأشياء قبل وقوعها واعترض عليه بأن العلم تابع للمعلوم على معنى أنهما يتطابقان والأصل في هذه المطابقة هو المعلوم ألا یری أن صورة الفرس مثلا على الجدار، إنما كانت على هذه الهيئة المخصوصة لأن الفرس في حد نفسه هكذا ولا يتصور أن ينعكس الحال بينهما، فالعلم بأن زيداً سيقوم غداً مثلاً إنما الصدور عن وهو ما نحن نمنعه، وأما وجوب ثواب المطيع بمقتضى الوعد فلا ننكره كيف وقد قال عز من قائل يبدل القول لدي ﴾ [ق: ۲۹] فإن قلت : دوام الترتب أو أكثريته شرط في العاديات كما مر ، فكيف يكون عقاب العاصي قلت : الأكثر بالنسبة إلى عامة المكلفين هو عقاب العاصي . قوله : (واعترض عليه بأن العلم تابع إلخ) قد يجاب عنه بأن المراد من الوجه الأول أن الله تعالى إذ علم في الأزل أن العبد يختار فيما لا يزال فعلاً معيناً فيتصف به لم يكن بد من وقوع هذا الاختيار والاتصاف المتفرع عليه فيما لا يزال وإلا لانقلب العلم جهلا، فتعلق علمه تعالى بوجود شيء يستلزم وجوده بنحو من استلزام السبب للسبب لا عكسه حتى يرد الاعتراض. يتحقق إذا كان هو في نفسه بحيث يقوم فيه دون العكس فلا مدخل للعلم في وجوب الفعل وامتناعه وسلب القدرة والاختيار وإلا لزم أن لا يكون تعالى فعلاً مختاراً لكونه عالما بأفعاله وجوداً وعدماً .

Checkpoint
According to the response, how does divine knowledge relate to the known?
A) It follows the known like human knowledge
B) It necessitates the known as a cause
C) It is independent of the known
D) It only applies to necessary things

Key Takeaway
Divine foreknowledge does not negate human free will because it actively necessitates the known, unlike passive human knowledge, and this account preserves both divine omniscience and human moral responsibility.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God's knowledge of your every choice necessitates that you make that choice, what does that mean for your sense of personal responsibility? Do you still feel accountable for your sins?
?
This lesson argues that divine knowledge is active, not passive. If that is true, then every moment of your life is as if God is 'causing' your choices. Does that comfort you or trouble you? How does it affect the way you repent?
?
The objector used a simple analogy of a painting. How often do you reduce God's attributes to human-like limitations? What would it mean for your prayer life if you truly believed that God's knowledge is the very ground of your actions?
