The Second Objection and Its Naqḍ
Second objection and its rebuttal by analogy to God's own actions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second Ashʿarite objection that whatever God wills from human actions necessarily occurs, and the Muʿtazilite rebuttal by analogy to God's own actions.

Core Idea
If God's will suffices for occurrence, no created power matters.

The Ashʿarites argue that divine will determines all occurrences, leaving no room for human power. The Muʿtazilites counter with a logical rebuttal: if this were true, God Himself would lack power over His own acts. This lesson examines the objection and its initial rebuttal.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the first part of the passage: 'Whatever God wills to exist from the servant's actions occurs necessarily... so he has no power.'
Notice the claim of necessity.
2
Identify the rebuttal
The passage then says 'it is also rebutted by the counterexample of the Exalted Creator.' How does this objection apply to God?
The rebuttal uses analogy (naqḍ).
3
Reflect on the logic
Consider why the same logic would imply God has no power over His own acts. Is that acceptable?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
( الوجه الثاني : ما أراد الله وجوده ) من أفعال العبد ( وقع قطعاً وما أراد الله عدمه ( منها لم ( يقع قطعاً ) فلا قدرة له على شيء منهما أصلاً، ويرد عليه أيضاً النقض بالباري سبحانه وتعالى على أن المعتزلة ذاهبون على أن ما أراده الله أو أفعال نفسه كان كذلك بخلاف أفعال غيره . قوله : ( وما أراد عدمه إلخ) هاهنا واسطة وهي أن لا يريد الوجود ولا العدم لكنه متحقق عندنا وإن جوزه المعتزلة كما سيأتي، ثم الأولى أن يقول : وما لم يرد وجوده لم يقع قطعا لأن ما لم يقع قطعاً يكون عدمه أزلياً فذلك العدم ليس مراداً وإلا كان متجدداً. قوله : ( ويرد عليه النقض بالباري تعالى إلخ ) لأن ما أراد الله سبحانه من أفعال نفسه وقع، وما أراد عدمه لم يقع فلا قدرة، وأجيب بأن وجوب وقوع مراد الله تعالى بإرادته لا ينافي قادريته، وأما وجوب العبد أو امتناعه بإرادة الباري فمناف لقادرية العبد وأنت خبير بأن الكلام في المنافاة للقادرية بمعنى المتمكن من الفعل والترك والفرق حينئذ محل تأمل إلا أن يلتزم التسلسل في التعلقات كما مر غير مرة .
(The second aspect: What God wills to exist) from the servant's actions (occurs necessarily, and what God wills not to exist from them (does not occur necessarily) so he has no power over either of them at all, and it is also rebutted by the counterexample of the Exalted Creator, while the Mu'tazilites hold that what God wills of His own actions is such, unlike the actions of others. His saying: (And what He wills not to exist etc.) Here there is an intermediate state, which is that He does not will existence nor non-existence, but it is realized with us, though the Mu'tazilites permit it as will come. Then it is better to say: And what He does not will to exist does not occur necessarily, because what does not occur necessarily its non-existence is eternal, so that non-existence is not willed, otherwise it would be temporal. His saying: (And it is rebutted by the counterexample of the Exalted Creator etc.) Because what God wills from His own actions occurs, and what He wills not to exist does not occur, so there is no power. And it was answered that the necessary occurrence of what God wills by His will does not contradict His capability (qādīriyya). As for the necessity or impossibility of the servant's act by the will of God, it contradicts the capability of the servant, and you are aware that the discussion concerns the contradiction to capability in the sense of being capable of both act and omission, and the difference then is a place of reflection, unless one commits to an infinite regress in attachments (taʿalluqāt) as has been mentioned repeatedly.
Source pages p. 174

Commentary
This passage presents the second objection: Whatever God wills from the servant's actions necessarily occurs, and whatever He wills not to occur does not occur, so the servant has no power. The objection is then rebutted by pointing to God's own actions: if the same logic were applied, God would not have power either, since His will determines His acts. The commentary clarifies that there is an intermediate state where God neither wills existence nor non-existence (this is realized for the Ashʿarites, though Muʿtazilites allow it). The response distinguishes between God's capability (qādīriyya), which is not negated by His own will determining His acts, and the servant's capability, which is negated by God's will determining the servant's acts. The commentary notes that the difference between the two cases is a matter of reflection, unless one accepts an infinite regress in divine attachments.

Key Terms
إرادة
قدرة
نقض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that if God wills something, it necessarily happens, and that this leaves no room for any power, even God's.
Correction: The rebuttal shows that the same logic would deny God's power, which is false. The issue is not willing itself but the type of power involved. Ashʿarites affirm God's power is not negated by His will; Muʿtazilites argue that for humans, divine will negates human power.

Checkpoint
What is the rebuttal (naqḍ) to the second objection?
A) God's will is not universal
B) The same logic would deny God's power over His own acts
C) The servant has no will
D) The objection is invalid

Key Takeaway
The second objection is rebutted by showing it would also deny God's power, thus it cannot be sound as stated.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider that God's will is all-encompassing, do you truly believe your choices matter? How does this affect your daily decisions? Are you willing to take responsibility for your actions even if you feel they are determined?
?
If the Ashʿarite objection were correct, it would also deny God's power. Does this make you reconsider the relationship between God's will and your own? How can you live with both submission to God's will and a sense of personal accountability?
?
The Muʿtazilites defended human freedom by distinguishing God's actions from human actions. Do you find that distinction intellectually honest or convenient? What would it mean for your own relationship with God if you accepted that your actions are entirely your own?

The Response: Distinguishing Divine and Human Capability
Ashʿarite response distinguishing divine and human capability.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ashʿarite response to the Muʿtazilite rebuttal: that divine will does not negate God's capability but does negate human capability, and the resulting discussion about the infinite regress of attachments.

Core Idea
God's will determines everything, yet God remains capable; humans become incapable.

After the Muʿtazilite rebuttal, the Ashʿarites offer a nuanced response. They distinguish between God's own capability (qādīriyya) and human capability. While God's will determines His actions without negating His power, the same determines human actions and negates their power. The commentary also notes an intermediate state and the possibility of infinite regress. This lesson unpacks that response.

Guided Path
1
Read the response
Read the second part of the passage: 'And it was answered that the necessary occurrence of what God wills by His will does not contradict His capability...'
2
Compare the two capabilities
Notice the difference: God's capability is not negated, but the servant's capability is. Why? Because the servant's capability requires the possibility of doing otherwise.
The key is the definition of capability as being able to do or not do.
3
Consider the intermediate state
The passage mentions an intermediate state where God does not will existence nor non-existence. This is realized for Ashʿarites but allowed by Muʿtazilites.
This affects the logic of the objection.
4
Reflect on infinite regress
The commentary ends with a note that the difference between the cases may require accepting an infinite regress in divine attachments.
This is a deeper theological issue beyond this lesson.

( الوجه الثاني : ما أراد الله وجوده ) من أفعال العبد ( وقع قطعاً وما أراد الله عدمه ( منها لم ( يقع قطعاً ) فلا قدرة له على شيء منهما أصلاً، ويرد عليه أيضاً النقض بالباري سبحانه وتعالى على أن المعتزلة ذاهبون على أن ما أراده الله أو أفعال نفسه كان كذلك بخلاف أفعال غيره . قوله : ( وما أراد عدمه إلخ) هاهنا واسطة وهي أن لا يريد الوجود ولا العدم لكنه متحقق عندنا وإن جوزه المعتزلة كما سيأتي، ثم الأولى أن يقول : وما لم يرد وجوده لم يقع قطعا لأن ما لم يقع قطعاً يكون عدمه أزلياً فذلك العدم ليس مراداً وإلا كان متجدداً. قوله : ( ويرد عليه النقض بالباري تعالى إلخ ) لأن ما أراد الله سبحانه من أفعال نفسه وقع، وما أراد عدمه لم يقع فلا قدرة، وأجيب بأن وجوب وقوع مراد الله تعالى بإرادته لا ينافي قادريته، وأما وجوب العبد أو امتناعه بإرادة الباري فمناف لقادرية العبد وأنت خبير بأن الكلام في المنافاة للقادرية بمعنى المتمكن من الفعل والترك والفرق حينئذ محل تأمل إلا أن يلتزم التسلسل في التعلقات كما مر غير مرة .
(The second aspect: What God wills to exist) from the servant's actions (occurs necessarily, and what God wills not to exist from them (does not occur necessarily) so he has no power over either of them at all, and it is also rebutted by the counterexample of the Exalted Creator, while the Mu'tazilites hold that what God wills of His own actions is such, unlike the actions of others. His saying: (And what He wills not to exist etc.) Here there is an intermediate state, which is that He does not will existence nor non-existence, but it is realized with us, though the Mu'tazilites permit it as will come. Then it is better to say: And what He does not will to exist does not occur necessarily, because what does not occur necessarily its non-existence is eternal, so that non-existence is not willed, otherwise it would be temporal. His saying: (And it is rebutted by the counterexample of the Exalted Creator etc.) Because what God wills from His own actions occurs, and what He wills not to exist does not occur, so there is no power. And it was answered that the necessary occurrence of what God wills by His will does not contradict His capability (qādīriyya). As for the necessity or impossibility of the servant's act by the will of God, it contradicts the capability of the servant, and you are aware that the discussion concerns the contradiction to capability in the sense of being capable of both act and omission, and the difference then is a place of reflection, unless one commits to an infinite regress in attachments (taʿalluqāt) as has been mentioned repeatedly.
Source pages p. 174

Commentary
This passage presents the second objection: Whatever God wills from the servant's actions necessarily occurs, and whatever He wills not to occur does not occur, so the servant has no power. The objection is then rebutted by pointing to God's own actions: if the same logic were applied, God would not have power either, since His will determines His acts. The commentary clarifies that there is an intermediate state where God neither wills existence nor non-existence (this is realized for the Ashʿarites, though Muʿtazilites allow it). The response distinguishes between God's capability (qādīriyya), which is not negated by His own will determining His acts, and the servant's capability, which is negated by God's will determining the servant's acts. The commentary notes that the difference between the two cases is a matter of reflection, unless one accepts an infinite regress in divine attachments.

Key Terms
قاد رية
تعالق
إرادة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that if God's will determines everything, then nothing has any power, including God.
Correction: The response clarifies that God's own will does not negate His power because it is intrinsic, but it does negate created power because it is extrinsic.

Checkpoint
According to the response, why does God's will not negate His own capability?
A) Because God's will is not effective on Himself
B) Because God's will is part of His essence and does not conflict with His power
C) Because God has infinite power
D) Because the objection is invalid

Key Takeaway
The Ashʿarite response distinguishes between divine capability (unaffected by His will) and human capability (negated by His will), based on the definition of capability as the ability to do otherwise.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When was the last time you felt entitled to credit for a success or ashamed of a failure, forgetting that your capability to act was entirely contingent on Allah's will? How would your response change if you truly internalized that your ability to do or not do is negated by His will?
?
This lesson teaches that the distinction between God's capability and yours is subtle and requires accepting an infinite regress. Does this complexity challenge your faith, or does it deepen your awe of Allah's absolute sovereignty? What would you risk by simplifying this doctrine?
?
If you truly believed that your every action—good or bad—occurs only because Allah wills it, and that you have no independent power, what would you do differently tomorrow? Be specific: which habits or assumptions would you fight to change?

The Second Argument and the Intermediary Case
Presents the second argument and its commentary on the intermediary case.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second argument that God's will necessitates human actions and the nuance regarding the intermediary case where God wills neither existence nor non-existence.

Core Idea
God's will directly determines human actions, leaving no power to the servant.

This lesson presents the second argument in a series, which asserts that whatever God wills to occur from human actions necessarily happens. It also clarifies an important nuance: there is an intermediate case where God wills neither existence nor non-existence, but that is still realized. The commentator suggests a more precise phrasing.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Notice that it begins with the second argument and then moves to a commentary on a specific phrase.
The commentary discusses an intermediary case.
2
Identify the core argument
Find the statement: 'whatever God wills to exist from the actions of the servant occurs necessarily, and whatever He wills not to exist does not occur.' This is the key claim.
The argument ends with 'so he has no power over any of them at all.'
3
Understand the intermediary nuance
The commentator notes that there is an intermediate case where God wills neither existence nor non-existence. Understand why this case is still real according to the author.
The Mu'tazilites allow this possibility, as will be discussed later.
4
Reflect on the better phrasing
The commentator suggests that it is better to say 'what He did not will to exist does not occur' because non-existence is eternal and thus not a temporal volition.
This avoids implying that non-existence is willed temporally.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
( الوجه الثاني : ما أراد الله وجوده ) من أفعال العبد ( وقع قطعاً وما أراد الله عدمه ( منها لم ( يقع قطعاً ) فلا قدرة له على شيء منهما أصلاً، ويرد عليه أيضاً النقض بالباري سبحانه وتعالى على أن المعتزلة ذاهبون على أن ما أراده الله أو أفعال نفسه كان كذلك بخلاف أفعال غيره . قوله : ( وما أراد عدمه إلخ) هاهنا واسطة وهي أن لا يريد الوجود ولا العدم لكنه متحقق عندنا وإن جوزه المعتزلة كما سيأتي، ثم الأولى أن يقول : وما لم يرد وجوده لم يقع قطعا لأن ما لم يقع قطعاً يكون عدمه أزلياً فذلك العدم ليس مراداً وإلا كان متجدداً. قوله : ( ويرد عليه النقض بالباري تعالى إلخ ) لأن ما أراد الله سبحانه من أفعال نفسه وقع، وما أراد عدمه لم يقع فلا قدرة، وأجيب بأن وجوب وقوع مراد الله تعالى بإرادته لا ينافي قادريته، وأما وجوب العبد أو امتناعه بإرادة الباري فمناف لقادرية العبد وأنت خبير بأن الكلام في المنافاة للقادرية بمعنى المتمكن من الفعل والترك والفرق حينئذ محل تأمل إلا أن يلتزم التسلسل في التعلقات كما مر غير مرة .
The second point: What God wills to exist from the actions of the servant occurs necessarily, and what God wills not to exist from them does not occur necessarily, so he has no power over any of them at all. And it is also refuted by the analogy with the Exalted and Glorious God, because the Mu'tazilites hold that what God wills from His own actions is such, unlike the actions of others. He said: (And what He wills not to exist, etc.) Here there is an intermediary, which is that He does not will existence nor non-existence, but it is realized with us even if the Mu'tazilites allow it as will come. Then it is better to say: And what He did not will to exist does not occur necessarily, because what does not occur necessarily, its non-existence is eternal, so that non-existence is not willed, otherwise it would be temporal. He said: (And it is refuted by the analogy with the Exalted God, etc.) Because what God the Glorified wills from His own actions occurs, and what He wills not to exist does not occur, so there is no power. And it is answered that the necessary occurrence of what God wills by His will does not contradict His omnipotence. But the necessity of the servant or his impossibility by the will of the Creator contradicts the servant's capability. And you are aware that the discussion about contradiction of capability in the sense of being able to do and leave, the difference then is a matter of reflection, unless one commits to the chain of relations as has passed many times.
Source pages p. 174

Commentary
This passage presents the second argument in a series of proofs related to divine actions and human actions. The argument states that whatever God wills to exist from human actions necessarily occurs, and whatever He wills not to exist necessarily does not occur. Therefore, the human has no power over either occurrence or non-occurrence at all. The text then notes that this argument is refuted by analogy with God Himself: if God's will necessitates actions, then God's own actions would also be necessitated, implying He is not a free agent. The Mu'tazilites are mentioned as holding a distinction: God's will necessitates His own actions but not those of others. The commentary then explains two points. First, it clarifies a nuance: there is an intermediate case where God neither wills existence nor non-existence of a thing, but that case is still realized according to the author, though the Mu'tazilites allow it. The commentary suggests a better phrasing: 'what He did not will to exist does not occur' because if it never occurs, its non-existence is eternal and thus not a temporal volition. Second, the commentary addresses the analogy with God: the objection is that if God's will causes necessity in human acts, then by analogy, God's will would also cause necessity in His own acts, negating His free agency. The answer distinguishes: the necessary occurrence of God's willed acts by His own will does not contradict His omnipotence—He is the agent of that necessity. However, the necessity or impossibility of a servant's act due to God's will does contradict the servant's capability to act freely. The commentator notes that the contradiction is about being able to both do and leave the act, and the difference between God and servant on this point is a matter of reflection unless one accepts an infinite chain of relations (tasalsul) as previously discussed.

إرادة
قدرة
وجوب الفعل
امتناع الفعل
النقض بالباري

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the second argument, what happens to an action that God wills to exist?
A) It becomes possible but not necessary
B) It occurs necessarily
C) The servant gains full control over it
D) It becomes impossible

Key Takeaway
The second argument asserts that God's will directly determines human actions, leaving no power to the servant, with a nuance about the intermediary case.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God's will directly causes your every action, what does that mean for your sense of personal responsibility? Reflect on a recent sin you committed: could it be that you had no power to avoid it? How does this lesson challenge or confirm your belief about your own freedom?
?
The commentator mentions an intermediary case where God does not will an action's existence or non-existence, yet it is still real. Have you ever felt like your actions are in a 'gray area' – not clearly willed by God but still happening? How does this affect your trust in God's complete control?
?
Consider the statement 'what God did not will to exist does not occur necessarily.' Does this make you feel helpless? Or does it deepen your reliance on God? In what area of your life do you most need to submit to the reality that nothing occurs without His will?

The Objection of Analogy with God and Its Answer
Presents the objection of analogy with God and the answer distinguishing divine and human capability.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection to the second argument based on analogy with God, and the response that distinguishes divine and human capability.

Core Idea
The necessity caused by God's will does not negate His freedom but does negate human freedom.

The previous argument invited an objection: if God's will necessitates, then by analogy God's own actions would be necessitated, undermining His free agency. This lesson presents that objection and the answer that God's necessity by His own will is compatible with omnipotence, while human necessity contradicts capability.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection passage
Read the Arabic passage that begins with 'قوله : ( ويرد عليه النقض بالباري تعالى إلخ )'. Understand that this is the objection from analogy with God.
The objection claims that just as God's will necessitates human actions, it would also necessitate God's own actions.
2
Understand the Mu'tazilite position
The objection references the Mu'tazilites who distinguish between God's will regarding His own actions and His will regarding others' actions.
They hold that God's will necessitates His own actions but not others'.
3
Follow the answer
The answer states that the necessary occurrence of what God wills by His own will does not contradict His omnipotence. However, the necessity of the servant's action by God's will does contradict the servant's capability.
The distinction is crucial: God is the source of the necessity, so it does not limit Him.
4
Reflect on the commentator's remark
The commentator notes that the difference between God and servant regarding capability is a matter of deep reflection, and may require accepting an infinite chain of relations (tasalsul) as discussed previously.
This shows the depth of the discussion.

Denial of Generated Acts for God
Al-Jurjani presents the Mu'tazilite denial of generated acts for God and the refutation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazilite denial that God can produce generated acts, the arguments for and against, and how the refutations rely on the permissibility of direct causation and the distinction between sequence and causation.

Core Idea
God's acts need no generating cause; apparent causal sequences are merely customary.

This passage tackles a key Mu'tazilite objection: if God's acts could be generated, He would need a cause like humans do. Al-Jurjani presents the argument, its supporters, and the refutation drawn from earlier discussions. You'll see how the response uses analogies and empirical examples to dismantle the objection.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the full Arabic passage once to get the flow, then read the literal translation. Notice the structure: position, objection, two responses, empirical counterexample, and final reply.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the Mu'tazilite position and Abu Hashim's role
Find where the Mu'tazilites are quoted and where Abu Hashim agrees in one of his two opinions. Understand that Abu Hashim had two views.
3
Understand the objection: God would need a cause
The objection is that if God produced a generated act, He would need a generating cause, which is impossible for God. Note how the objection relies on the claim that generated acts cannot occur without a cause.
This is the core of the debate.
4
Analyze the two responses
First response: the generated act can occur directly, as shown in the first branch. Second response: the need for a cause is analogous to the need for substances for accidents, which is accepted. Then the empirical example (wind moving branches) and the final answer that sequence is merely customary.
Focus on how each response refutes the necessity of a cause.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني : ( من الفروع ( قد منع بعض المعتزلة من ثبوت الفعل المتولد لله تعالى بل جمع فعله ( عندهم ( مباشرة ) ومقدور بالقادرية من غير توسط سبب ( ووافقهم عليه أبو هاشم في أحد قوليه وإلا احتاج في فعله إلى سبب ) هو المولد لذلك الفعل كاحتياج العباد إلى أسباب المتولدات وهو على الله محال، ) والجواب : إن ذلك ) أي لزوم احتياج الباري بناء على امتناع وقوع الفعل ) المتولد (بدون السببب ) ، وقد عرفت بطلانه بما أوردناه على الفرع الأول من جواز وقوع المتولد من فعل العبد مباشراً له وقد قال به أبو هاشم أيضاً في الغائب في أحد قوليه وإن منعه في الشاهد مطلقاً ( مع أنه ( أي الاحتياج إلى السبب المولد ( لا يزيد على امتناع وجود الأعراض بدون محالها، إذ هاهنا أيضاً يلزم احتياجه في إيجاد الأعراض إلى إيجاد الجواهر فما هو العذر هناك هو العذر ،هاهنا والتحقيق أنه لا محذور لأن الاحتياج في الحقيقة راجع إلى الفعل المتولد والعرض ) وجوزه بعضهم وافقهم أبو هاشم في القول الآخر لما يحكم) ويشهد به الحس من حركة الأغصان والأوراق على الأشجار بحركة الرياح العاصفة ) واعتمادها عليها ) ولا شك أن حركة الرياح واعتمادها ( من فعل الله تعالى بالمباشرة ) فتكون حركة الأغصان والأوراق من توليداً ( والجواب : ما سبق في فعل العبد من أن ترتب فعل على آخر لا يستلزم أن يكون مسبباً له لجواز أن يكون الجميع بقدرة الله تعالى ابتداءً ويكون الترتب بمجرد إجراء العادة
The second: (of the branches) (Some Mu'tazilites have denied the existence of the generated act for God Most High; rather, all His acts (according to them) are direct and accomplished by divine power without the intermediation of a cause. (Abu Hashim agreed with them in one of his two opinions; otherwise He would need in His act a cause) that is the generator of that act, like the need of servants for the causes of generated things, and that is impossible for God. (And the answer: that) i.e., the necessity of the Creator's need based on the impossibility of the occurrence of the generated act without the cause (and you have known its invalidity by what we presented on the first branch of the permissibility of the occurrence of the generated from the act of the servant as direct for him, and Abu Hashim also said this regarding the unseen in one of his two opinions, although he absolutely denied it in the witnessed. (Moreover, this need for a generating cause is no greater than the impossibility of the existence of accidents without their substrates; for here too it would necessitate His need in creating accidents to create substances, so what is the excuse there is the excuse here. And the verification is that there is no harm, because the need in reality reverts to the generated act and the accident.) (And some permitted it, and Abu Hashim agreed with them in the other opinion, because) what is judged and witnessed by sense perception from the motion of branches and leaves on trees due to the movement of violent winds and their dependence on them, and there is no doubt that the movement of the winds and their dependence (is from the direct act of God Most High), so the motion of branches and leaves would be generated from it. (And the answer: what preceded regarding the act of the servant, that the ordering of one act after another does not necessitate that it be a cause for it, because it is possible that all are by the power of God Most High from the beginning, and the ordering is merely by custom.)
Source pages p. 182

Commentary
This passage addresses the second branch. Some Mu'tazilites denied that God can bring about a generated act; they held all His acts are direct. Abu Hashim agreed in one of his two opinions, arguing that otherwise God would need a generating cause like humans do, which is impossible for Him. The answer is that this necessity was already refuted in the first branch, where it was shown that the generated act can occur directly (by human power). The need for a cause is parallel to the need for substances for accidents, which is accepted. Some allowed generated acts for God based on empirical examples like wind moving branches, but the answer is that sequence does not entail causation; it is merely customary ordering.

Key Terms
الفعل المتولد
المباشرة
السبب المولد
أبو هاشم
احتياج الباري

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God must act through causes like humans do; otherwise He would need a cause.
Correction: God can produce acts directly; causal sequences are merely customary, not necessary.

Checkpoint
What was Abu Hashim's position on God's generated acts according to this passage?
A) He absolutely denied any generated act for God.
B) He affirmed the possibility of generated acts for God in one of his two opinions.
C) He said God's acts are always generated.
D) He was silent on the issue.

Key Takeaway
God's acts are direct; apparent causal sequences in the world are merely customary, not necessitating a generating cause.

You habitually rely on 'causes'—effort, means, people—as if they create outcomes. If you truly internalized that God creates everything directly, what would you stop worrying about? What would you start trusting? Name one specific worry you will surrender to God after this lesson.

Sixth Branch: Death from a Wound – Generated or Divine Act?
Debate on death from a wound as a generated effect.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the theological debate about whether death resulting from a wound is a generated (mutawallad) effect, and the contradictions each position entails.

Core Idea
Can a wound cause death? The debate exposes a tension between causal generation and divine omnipotence.

This lesson delves into a specific branch of Kalam: whether death that follows a wound is 'generated' (mutawallad) from the wound's instruments. The discussion reveals a conflict between the principle of generation (tawleed) and the consensus that only God independently causes death and life. Let's explore the arguments.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice the two opposing positions.
2
Identify the two positions
The denier says death is not generated; the affirmer says it is. Each contradicts something.
3
Understand the contradictions
The denier contradicts his own principle of generation; the affirmer contradicts consensus and scripture.
4
Reflect on the theological significance
Consider how this debate reflects the broader Ash'ari concern for divine omnipotence over secondary causality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(السادس: اختلفوا في الموت المتولد من الجرح أي الحاصل عقيبه هل هو متولد من الآلات المتولدة من الجرح فنفاه قوم وأثبته آخرون، والنافي له مراغم لأصله في التوليد لأن ترتب الموت على الآلام يقتضي تولده منها كما في سائر المتولدات والمثبت له مراغم للإجماع فإن الأمة أجمعوا على أن المستقل بالإماتة والإحياء هو الله سبحانه وتعالى وللكتاب فإن نصوصه دالة عليه، قال تعالى : وهو يحيي ويميت (الأعراف : 158) ربي الذي يحي ويميت (البقرة : 258).
The sixth: They differed concerning death that is generated from a wound—i.e., occurring after it—whether it is generated from the instruments (ālāt) generated from the wound. Some denied it and others affirmed it. The denier contradicts his own principle (aṣl) in generation, because the succession of death upon pains necessitates its generation from them, as in other generated things. And the affirmer contradicts the consensus (ijmāʿ), for the community agreed that the independent agent in causing death and giving life is God, exalted is He, and the Book (al-kitāb), for its texts indicate it. He said, the Exalted: 'And He gives life and causes death' (Q. 7:158); 'My Lord who gives life and causes death' (Q. 2:258).
Source pages p. 184

Commentary
This passage presents a debate about whether death resulting from a wound is a generated effect (mutawallad) from the wound's instruments. Two positions exist: denial and affirmation. The denier contradicts his own principle of generation because if death follows pain, it should be generated like other effects. The affirmer contradicts consensus (ijmāʿ) and scripture, since the community agrees that God alone is the independent agent in causing death and giving life, supported by Qur'anic verses. The passage thus highlights the theological tension between the causal principle of tawleed and divine omnipotence over life and death.

Correction: This discussion shows it conflicts with divine exclusive agency; the Ash'ari position maintains that only God independently causes death and life.

Third Gloss: Independence in Life and Death
Gloss explaining the inclusion of life in the consensus argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the mention of giving life in the consensus argument is essential to expose the logical consequence of the Muʿtazilī position on power and opposites.

Core Idea
Including life in the argument is not incidental but a logical necessity.

This gloss clarifies why the earlier argument mentioned both causing death and giving life. It shows that for some Muʿtazila, if death can be generated by humans, then life must also be within human power, given their view that power relates equally to opposites. This insight strengthens the consensus that only Allah is independent in these acts.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice how the author presents two possible reasons for mentioning life.
The passage is a gloss on a previous statement.
2
Identify the two explanations
The gloss offers two reasons: either it is a digression, or it is necessary because some Muʿtazila would also violate consensus on life.
Focus on the second reason as it carries the main point.
3
Understand the Muʿtazilī view on power
Some Muʿtazila, like al-Jubbāʾī, hold that power relates equally to opposites. If true, then if one can cause death, one can also cause life.
This is the key premise that leads to the entailment.
4
Grasp the logical consequence
If a person can generate death (e.g., by a wound), then consistency demands they can also generate life. This would violate the consensus that only Allah gives life.
Thus mentioning life is not a digression but a necessary refutation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله على أن المستقل بالإماتة والإحياء إلخ ) ذكر الإحياء في صدد بيان لزوم خرق الإجماع إما على سبيل الاستطراد وإما إلى أنه يلزم بعض المعتزلة خرق الإجماع على استقلاله تعالى بالإيحاء أيضاً، لأن منهم من يدعي أن نسبة القدرة إلى الضدين على السوية كالجبائي فإذا اعترف بكون الموت المتولد من الجرح مقدوراً للجارح لزمه أن يعترف بأن الحياة أيضاً مقدورة له .
His statement: 'That the independent agent in causing death and giving life, etc.'—he mentioned giving life (iḥyāʾ) in the context of explaining the necessity of violating consensus, either as a digression or because it entails that some Muʿtazila violate consensus regarding His, the Exalted, being independent in giving life as well, for among them are those who claim that the relation of power to opposites is equal, like al-Jubbāʾī. So if he acknowledges that death generated from a wound is within the power of the wounder, then he must acknowledge that life is also within his power.
Source pages p. 184

Commentary
This gloss explains why the earlier argument mentioned both causing death and giving life. It suggests that including life may be a digression or necessary because some Muʿtazila (like al-Jubbāʾī) hold that power relates equally to opposites. If they admit that generated death is within human power, consistency would require that life also be within human power, thus violating the consensus that only God is independent in both.

Key Terms
الإماتة (imāta)
الإحياء (iḥyāʾ)
الإجماع (ijmāʿ)
القدرة (qudra)
الضدان (ḍiddān)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The mention of life in the consensus argument is an incidental digression without theological significance.
Correction: The gloss shows that mentioning life is crucial because it reveals that for some Muʿtazila, accepting human-generated death logically entails accepting human-generated life, thereby violating the consensus on God's exclusive power over life.

Checkpoint
Why does the gloss argue that affirming generated death leads to affirming generated life?
A) Because death and life are opposites
B) Because power relates equally to both in some Muʿtazilī views
C) Because the wound is the cause of both
D) Because consensus is not binding

Key Takeaway
Including life in the consensus argument is not incidental but necessary to expose the logical consequence of the Muʿtazilī view that power relates equally to opposites.

The Verse Indicating Divine Exclusivity in Life and Death
Gloss interpreting the verse 'He gives life and causes death' as indicating all such acts are from God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Qur'anic verse 'He gives life and causes death' is interpreted to affirm that all acts of life and death come exclusively from God.

Core Idea
The verse attributes all life and death solely to God.

This gloss unpacks a key Qur'anic verse used to support the doctrine that God alone is the exclusive agent of life and death. The commentator draws on a linguistic parallel with the common phrase 'so-and-so gives and withholds' to show that the verse implies universal and exclusive attribution. Let's examine the text and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the gloss text and translation
Start by reading the Arabic text and its literal translation. Notice the structure: the verse is quoted, then the interpretation is given, followed by a linguistic analogy.
The gloss is concise; every word carries weight.
2
Understand the linguistic analogy
The commentator mentions the common expression 'so-and-so gives and withholds.' Just as that phrase implies all giving and withholding are from that person, the verse implies all life and death are from God.
This analogy is drawn from the books of semantics (maʿānī).
3
Identify the theological implication
The clear conclusion is that God is the sole independent agent in granting life and causing death. This supports the Islamic doctrine of divine exclusivity in acts of creation and destruction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (قال الله تعالى هو يحيي ويميت) فإن المستفاد من الآية صدور جميع الإحياء والإماتة منه تعالى كما عرفت من قولهم فلان يعطي ويمنع على ما حقق في كتب المعاني .
His statement: 'God, the Exalted, said: He gives life and causes death'—for what is understood from the verse is that all instances of giving life and causing death issue from Him, the Exalted, as you have known from their saying 'so-and-so gives and withholds,' as established in the books of semantics (maʿānī).
Source pages p. 184

Commentary
This gloss explains that the Qur'anic verse 'He gives life and causes death' (Q. 7:158, 2:258) is interpreted to mean that all acts of giving life and causing death come exclusively from God. It draws an analogy with the common expression 'so-and-so gives and withholds,' which implies that all giving and withholding are attributed to that person. Similarly, the verse attributes all life and death to God alone, supporting the consensus that God is the sole independent agent.

Key Terms
الإحياء (iḥyāʾ)
الإماتة (imāta)
الآية (āya)
المعاني (maʿānī)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the verse only describes one of God's attributes, not that it indicates exclusive and all-encompassing agency.
Correction: The gloss clarifies that the verse is understood to mean that all instances of life and death issue from God, i.e., He is the exclusive agent.

Checkpoint

C) Only death is from God
D) The verse is metaphorical

Key Takeaway
The verse 'He gives life and causes death' is interpreted to mean that all acts of life and death come exclusively from God, as shown by the linguistic analogy with 'gives and withholds'.

Can God Cause Pain Without Weakness?
Al-Jurjani discusses the ninth branch and its dependence on the second branch, with marginal clarifications.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the debate about whether God can cause pain without an intermediate state of weakness and see that this branch may be redundant or independent depending on how generation is interpreted.

Core Idea
Whether God causes pain directly or through weakness depends on accepting generation in divine acts.

This lesson examines the ninth branch concerning divine causation of pain. Al-Jurjani frames it as dependent on the second branch about generation. The discussion reveals a subtle point: the proper phrasing of the question changes whether it is a separate issue. Let's explore the arguments and the commentator's clarifications.

Guided Path
1
Understand the context
Recognize that the ninth branch is the last of the branches discussed in the book and builds on the second branch about generation (tawlid).
The second branch likely dealt with whether God's acts can be generated through means.
2
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation, noting the distinction between the main text and the marginal comments (e.g., 'qawlahu').
The passage contains clarifications about wording and references to al-Amidi and al-Abkar.
3
Analyze the two positions
Identify the two positions: those who deny generation in God's acts say pain is not caused by weakness; those who affirm generation say pain can be generated from weakness.
4
Reflect on the redundancy
Consider why al-Amidi omitted this branch: because if phrased correctly, it is a subset of the second branch. Also consider the alternative interpretation that makes it independent.
The alternative interpretation shifts the question to whether God can cause pain directly, contrary to observed concomitance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
التاسع : وهو آخر الفروع المذكورة في الكتاب هل يمكن إحداث الألم بلا وهي من الله تعالى أم لا ؟ هذا على ما مبني تقدم في الفرع الثاني، فمن لم يجوز أن يكون فعله تعالى متولداً حكم بأن الألم الصادر عنه تعالى لايكون بسبب الوهي وتوليده إياه ومن جوز التوليد في أفعاله جوز كون الألم الصادر عنه متولداً من الوهي ويعلم من كونه مبنيا على الفرع الثاني أن العبارة الظاهرة هاهنا أن يقال: هل يمكن من الله تعالى إحداث الألم بالوهي أو لا وحينئذ يكون جزئياً من جزئيات الفرع الثاني فلا حاجة إلى أفراده ولذلك لم يذكره الآمدي . قوله : (المذكورة في الكتاب) فيه إيماء إلى أنه ليس آخر الفروع المذكورة في الأبكار فإن فيه فروعاً آخر. قوله : (هل يمكن إحداث الألم يمكن أن يقال: حاصله أن الواقع فيما نشاهده هو وقوع الألم من الله تعالى مقارناً للوهي، فهل يجوز خلاف ما نشاهده بأن يحدث الألم بلا وهاء أم لا؟ فمن قال : بأن الترتب في أفعال الله تعالى يدل على التوليد وبأنه لا يجوز وقوع المتولد مباشراً لم يجوز ومن لم يقل جوز فالعبارة الظاهرة على هذا التوجيه هي التي وقعت في الكتاب لكن لا يكون هذا الفرع حينئذ مبنياً على مجرد الفرع الثاني كما لا يخفى.
Ninth: It is the last of the branches mentioned in the book: Is it possible for God to cause pain without weakness or not? This is built upon what preceded in the second branch. For whoever does not allow that His act is generated rules that the pain emanating from Him is not due to weakness and His generating it. And whoever allows generation in His acts allows pain emanating from Him to be generated from weakness. And it is known from its being built on the second branch that the apparent wording here is to say: Is it possible from God to cause pain by weakness or not? And then it is a particular of the particulars of the second branch, so there is no need to single it out, and therefore al-Amidi did not mention it. His saying: 'mentioned in the book'—there is an indication that it is not the last of the branches mentioned in al-Abkar, for it contains other branches. His saying: 'Is it possible to cause pain'—it can be said: Its summary is that what occurs in what we observe is the occurrence of pain from God concomitant with weakness. So, is it permissible to contradict what we observe by causing pain without weakness or not? Whoever says that the concomitance in God's acts indicates generation and that it is not permissible for the generated to occur directly would not allow it, and whoever does not say so would allow it. Thus the apparent wording according to this interpretation is what occurred in the book, but then this branch is not built merely on the second branch, as is not hidden.
Source pages p. 186

Commentary
This passage introduces the ninth and final branch (according to the book) concerning whether God can cause pain without an intermediate state of weakness (wahi). The issue is framed as dependent on an earlier discussion (the second branch) about whether generation can occur in God's acts. If one denies generation in God's acts, then pain from God is not via weakness and generation; if one affirms generation, then pain can be generated from weakness. The commentator notes that the proper formulation of the question should be whether God can cause pain 'by weakness' or not, making it a subset of the second branch and thus redundant—this is why al-Amidi omitted it. The marginal comments elaborate: the book's phrasing can be interpreted as asking whether God can cause pain without weakness (i.e., directly), contrary to observed concomitance. Those who see the observed concomitance as evidence of generation forbid direct causation; those who do not see it as evidence allow it. Under that interpretation, the branch is not merely derived from the second branch but is a distinct issue. This clarifies a potential confusion about the independence of this branch.

Key Terms
الألم
الوهي
توليد
الله تعالى
الفرع الثاني

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The ninth branch is simply a subset of the second branch and has no independent significance.
Correction: The marginal comments show that depending on the interpretation of the question, the ninth branch can be seen as an independent inquiry into whether God can cause pain directly, contradicting observed concomitance.

Checkpoint
Why did al-Amidi omit the ninth branch according to the main text?
A) He forgot
B) Because it is a particular of the second branch and thus redundant
C) He disagreed with the premise
D) The branch is too long

Key Takeaway
The ninth branch question hinges on whether we allow generation in divine acts, and its independence is contested depending on the phrasing of the question.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you experience pain, do you immediately look for a worldly cause, or do you pause to consider that God may be directly causing it without any intermediate? How does this lesson challenge you to rethink your response to suffering?
?

?
The debate about generation in divine acts might seem abstract, but it touches on how you perceive God's involvement in your life. Do you believe that God's act of causing pain is immediate or mediated? What difference does your belief make in how you cope with trials?

The Sunni and Mu'tazili Views on Tawfiq and Hidaya
Definitions of tawfiq and hidaya with Sunni and Mu'tazili views.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Sunni (Ash'ari) and Mu'tazili interpretations of tawfiq (divine assistance) and hidaya (guidance), including the internal Ash'ari debate between al-Ash'ari and al-Juwayni on the nature of tawfiq.

Core Idea
Tawfiq is either creating ability to obey or creating obedience itself; hidaya is either creating faith or merely calling to it.

In this passage, the author introduces the second issue concerning terms that the Mu'tazila interpret metaphorically: tawfiq and hidaya. The Sunni view, represented by al-Ash'ari and most of his followers, defines tawfiq as the creation of ability to obey, while Imam al-Haramayn al-Juwayni defines it as the creation of obedience itself. For hidaya, the Sunnis take it in its real meaning of creating guidance (faith), whereas the Mu'tazila interpret both as merely the call to faith and clarification of the way.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once. Notice how it first defines tawfiq according to al-Ash'ari, then presents al-Juwayni's alternative, then defines hidaya, and finally states the Mu'tazili interpretation.
The Mu'tazili view is applied to both terms (أولوهما).
2
Compare the two Ash'ari views on tawfiq
Identify the difference: al-Ash'ari says tawfiq is creating ability (qudra) to obey; al-Juwayni says it is creating obedience itself. The commentary explains why al-Juwayni rejected the ability-based definition.
This relates to the Ash'ari theory of acquisition (kasb).
3
Understand the Mu'tazili position
The Mu'tazila reduce tawfiq and hidaya to external actions: calling (da'wa) and clarifying the path. They deny that Allah creates faith directly.
This fits their doctrine of human free will.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني : ( من تلك الأمور التي يؤولونها ( التوفيق والهداية ) ) فإن الشارح الأشعري وأكثر الأئمة من أصحابه حملوا التوفيق على خلق القدرة على الطاعة وهو مناسب للوضع اللغوي لأن الموافقة إنما هي بالطاعة ويخلق القدرة الحادثة على الطاعة يحصل الموافقة وقال إمام الحرمين : التوفيق خلق الطاعة لا خلق القدرة، إذ لا تأثير لها وحملوا الهداية على معناها الحقيقي أعني خلق الاهتداء وهو الإيمان والمعتزلة ( أولوهما بالدعوة إلى الإيمان والطاعة ) وإيضاح سبل
قوله : (لا يقتضي امتناع (الإيمان وإلا كان قبيحاً لا يسند إليه تعالى عندهم وقد يناقش في ذلك بأن ما ذكر يصلح سبباً للامتناع باعتبار أن مبدأ الوسم والمنع صفة في قلوبهم مانعة فتأمل. قوله : ( وقال إمام الحرمين التوفيق خلق الطاعة هذا مخالف لما نقل عن المصنف من أنه يوافق الحكماء في وقوع فعل العبد إذا كان اختيارياً بقدرته وموافق لما مر من المقاصد .
قوله : ( وحملوا الهداية على معناه الحقيقي قيل عليه هذا مع قوله والمعتزلة أولوهما يشير إلى أن الهداية حقيقة في خلق الاهتداء باتفاق الفريقين وتفسيرها بالدلالة تأويل فالبحث عنها بأنها باقية على حقيقتها أو مؤولة بحث كلامي وهذا يخالف ما ذكره في حاشية شرح المطالع أنه حقيقة في الدلالة على ما يوصل إلى المطلوب، واحتج عليه بقوله تعالى: {وأما ثمود فهديناهم فاستحبوا العمى} [فصلت: 17] على الهدى والجواب أن الغرض هاهنا بيان الحقيقة الشرعية المرادة في أغلب استعمالات الشارع، والمذكور في حواشي شرح المطالع معناه اللغوي أو العرفي فلا مخالفة، ويمكن أن يدفع أيضا بأن الهداية قد تتعدى إلى المفعول الثاني بنفسها، وقد تتعدى بواسطة الحرف وقد يفرق بينهما بأن معنى الأول الإيصال إلى المطلوب ولا يكون إلا فعل الله تعالى فلا يسند إلا إليه ومعنى الثاني الدلالة على ما يوصل فيسند إلى القرآن تارة نحو قوله تعالى : {يهدي للتي هي أقوم} [الإسراء: 9 ] وإلى النبي، أخرى نحو {إنك لتهدي إلى صراط مستقيم} [الشورى: 52]، والمراد بالهداية في قوله هاهنا وحملوا الهداية إلخ هو المتعدي بنفسه وفي حواشي المطالع هو المتعدي بالحرف فإنه حمل الهداية في قول
Second: (Among those matters that they interpret metaphorically are al-tawfiq (divine assistance) and al-hidaya (guidance).) For al-Ash'ari and most of the imams among his followers interpreted al-tawfiq as the creation of ability (qudra) to obey, which is suitable to the linguistic usage because agreement (muwafaqa) is only through obedience, and the creation of the contingent ability to obey brings about agreement. Imam al-Haramayn said: al-tawfiq is the creation of obedience, not the creation of ability, since ability has no effect. And they interpreted al-hidaya according to its real meaning, namely the creation of guidance (al-istihda') which is faith. The Mu'tazila interpreted them both as the call to faith and obedience and the clarification of ways. His statement: 'does not necessitate the impossibility of faith; otherwise it would be ugly, not attributed to Him, may He be exalted, according to them.' And it may be debated that what is mentioned is suitable as a cause of impossibility considering that the principle of branding and prevention is a preventing attribute in their hearts, so reflect. His statement: 'And Imam al-Haramayn said: al-tawfiq is the creation of obedience' contradicts what was transmitted from the author that he agrees with the philosophers regarding the occurrence of the servant's act when it is voluntary by his ability, and it agrees with what passed from the subjects. His statement: 'And they interpreted al-hidaya according to its real meaning' – it was objected: this, along with his statement 'and the Mu'tazila interpreted them both', indicates that al-hidaya is truly the creation of guidance by agreement of the two groups, and its interpretation as indication (dalala) is figurative, so the investigation of whether it remains on its true meaning or is interpreted is a theological investigation. This contradicts what he mentioned in the gloss on Sharh al-Matali' that it is truly indication to what leads to the desired goal. And he adduced as evidence His statement {And as for Thamud, We guided them, but they preferred blindness} [41:17] over guidance. The answer is that the purpose here is to explain the religious (shar'i) meaning intended in most usages of the Lawgiver, while what is mentioned in the glosses of Sharh al-Matali' is its linguistic or customary meaning, so there is no contradiction. And it can also be rebutted that hidaya sometimes takes a second object directly (by itself) and sometimes takes it via a preposition. It is said there is a distinction between them: the meaning of the first is reaching the goal, which is only an act of Allah, so it is only attributed to Him; the meaning of the second is indication of what leads, so it is attributed to the Qur'an sometimes, as in His statement {It guides to that which is most correct} [17:9], and to the Prophet at other times, as in {Indeed, you guide to a straight path} [42:52]. And the intended meaning of hidaya in his statement here 'and they interpreted hidaya' etc. is the one that takes a second object directly, while in the glosses of al-Matali' it is the one taking it via a preposition, for he interpreted hidaya in the saying...
Source pages p. 188

Commentary
This passage discusses the second issue: two key terms that the Mu'tazila interpret metaphorically: التوفيق (divine assistance) and الهداية (guidance). It first states the Sunni (Ash'ari) view: al-Shaykh Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari and most of his followers interpret التوفيق as the creation of ability (القدرة) to obey – since linguistic 'agreement' (موافقة) occurs through obedience, creating the ability brings about that agreement. However, Imam al-Haramayn al-Juwayni held that التوفيق is the creation of obedience itself, not ability, because ability has no effect (a reference to the Ash'ari view of acquisition, kasb). For الهداية, the Sunnis take it in its real meaning: creation of guidance (الاهتداء) which is faith. The Mu'tazila interpret both as merely 'calling to faith and obedience' and 'clarifying the ways' – i.e., merely providing guidance, not creating it. The passage then includes several marginal notes. The first note comments on a previous point (about the first issue) that the Mu'tazili objection about impossibility of faith does not hold because what they mention (marks, withholding) could still be a cause of prevention if considered as a preventing attribute in the heart. The second note points out an inconsistency: al-Juwayni's view that التوفيق is creation of obedience contradicts the author's usual agreement with the philosophers that the servant's act occurs by his own ability. The third note is a detailed discussion about the meaning of الهداية. The author had earlier said that الهداية is interpreted by Sunnis as creation of guidance (the real meaning), implying that 'guidance as indication' is metaphorical. This seems to conflict with a gloss on Sharh al-Matali' where الهداية is said to be truly 'indication to the desired goal'. The passage reconciles this: the context here is about the religious (shar'i) meaning used most often in revelation, while the gloss discusses the linguistic or customary meaning – so no contradiction. Furthermore, a linguistic distinction is made: الهداية can be transitive directly (to the second object), meaning 'to cause to reach the goal', which is only done by Allah; or transitive via a preposition (هدى إلى), meaning 'to indicate', which can be done by the Quran and the Prophet. The author clarifies that the الهداية discussed in this section is the first type (direct, causing to reach), while the gloss on al-Matali' discusses the second type (indicating). The passage ends mid-sentence as it continues onto the next page.

Key Terms
التوفيق
الهداية
القدرة
الطاعة
خلق الاهتداء
الدعوة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to al-Ash'ari, what is the meaning of tawfiq?
A) The creation of obedience itself
B) The creation of ability to obey
C) The call to faith and obedience
D) The clarification of the ways

Key Takeaway
The Sunnis define tawfiq as creating ability or obedience, and hidaya as creating faith, while the Mu'tazila reduce both to external guidance and calls.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you pray or do good deeds, do you ever feel that your ability to do so is a gift from Allah, or do you take it for granted? How would your worship change if you truly believed that every act of obedience is created by Him?
?
If the Mu'tazila are wrong and hidaya is not just a call but an actual creation of faith by Allah, then what does that mean for those who do not believe? Does this make you more humble about your own faith, knowing it was created in you?
?
The passage shows that even among Sunnis, there is a difference: tawfiq is either creating ability or creating obedience. Which view makes you feel more dependent on Allah? Could you honestly say that you rely on His creation of both your ability and your action?

The Linguistic Depth of Hidaya: Real vs. Metaphorical
Detailed analysis of hidaya: reconciling conflicting interpretations via a linguistic distinction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the debate over whether hidaya is truly 'creation of guidance' or merely 'indication', and the linguistic distinction between transitive direct (causing to reach) and prepositional (indicating) uses that resolves apparent contradictions.

Core Idea
Hidaya has two types: one causes reaching the goal (only Allah), the other indicates the way (Quran and Prophet).

This passage delves into a deeper controversy about the meaning of hidaya. The author had implied that hidaya is literally 'creation of guidance' and its interpretation as 'indication' is metaphorical. However, this seems to conflict with a gloss on Sharh al-Matali' where hidaya is taken as real indication. The passage resolves this by distinguishing between two linguistic forms of hidaya: one transitive directly (meaning to cause to reach) and one transitive via a preposition (meaning to indicate). This distinction preserves the divine exclusivity of true guidance while allowing human agents to be called guides.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on the objection and response
Notice the objection: the author's earlier statement contradicts a gloss. Read the response carefully.
Pay attention to the distinction between shar'i and linguistic meanings.
2
Identify the two types of hidaya
Find where the passage explains the difference between hidaya that takes a direct object (المتعدي بنفسه) and that which takes a preposition (المتعدي بالحرف).
The first means 'to cause to reach', the second 'to indicate'.
3
Apply the distinction to the examples
Notice how the Quran and Prophet are described as guides using the prepositional form, while Allah alone is the direct guide.
This reconciles the different usages.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That hidaya has only one meaning and that either it is always creation of guidance or always indication.
Correction: Hidaya has two distinct meanings depending on its grammatical construction: direct transitive (causing to reach) is exclusive to Allah, while prepositional (indicating) can be done by others.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the meaning of hidaya when it takes a second object directly (المتعدي بنفسه)?
A) Indication of the way
B) Calling to faith
C) Causing to reach the goal
D) Creation of ability

Key Takeaway
Hidaya is not a single concept; linguistically it divides into direct guidance (causing to reach, only from Allah) and indirect guidance (indicating, from Quran and Prophet), resolving apparent contradictions between texts.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read the Quran or listen to a sermon, do you assume that hearing guidance (indirect) is enough to change you? Do you realize that without Allah's direct guidance (causing to reach), your heart remains blind? What will you do today to beseech Allah for the direct type of hidaya?
?
The Prophet ﷺ could only indicate the path; he could not force faith. How does this distinction humble your expectations of your own efforts in da'wah? Are you relying on your own persuasion or trusting Allah's exclusive power to cause guidance?
?
If Allah gave Thamud guidance (indirectly) yet they chose blindness, what does that say about your own accountability? Are you treating Allah's signs as mere information, or as a call that demands immediate surrender?

Tawfiq and Hidaya: Definitions and Sectarian Views
Defines tawfiq and hidaya, presents Ash'ari and Mu'tazili views.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari definition of tawfiq and hidaya, including the nuance of Imam al-Haramayn, and the Mu'tazili alternative interpretation.

Core Idea
Divine enabling and guidance are creative acts of God, not mere invitation.

In this lesson, we examine the theological debate over the meaning of tawfiq (divine enabling) and hidaya (guidance). The Ash'ari school defines these as God's creation of ability and faith, while the Mu'tazila reduce them to mere invitation. This distinction is foundational for understanding divine action and human responsibility.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on definitions
Read the Arabic passage that presents the Ash'ari and Mu'tazili views. Focus on the key terms.
Notice that tawfiq is linked to creating ability, and hidaya to creating faith.
2
Compare two Ash'ari views
The main view interprets tawfiq as creating ability to obey; Imam al-Haramayn says it is creating obedience itself. How do they differ?
3
Identify the Mu'tazili view
The Mu'tazila interpret both tawfiq and hidaya as invitation (da'wa). Understand why they prefer this interpretation.
4
Reflect on linguistic connection
Note that tawfiq is from 'muwafaqa' (compliance), which fits the idea of enabling obedience.

ويخلق القدرة الحادثة على الطاعة يحصل الموافقة وقال إمام الحرمين : التوفيق خلق الطاعة لا خلق القدرة، إذ لا تأثير لها وحملوا الهداية على معناها الحقيقي أعني خلق الاهتداء وهو الإيمان والمعتزلة ( أولوهما بالدعوة إلى الإيمان والطاعة ) وإيضاح سبل المقاصد . قوله : ( وحملوا الهداية على معناه الحقيقي قيل عليه هذا مع قوله والمعتزلة أولوهما يشير إلى أن الهداية حقيقة في خلق الاهتداء باتفاق الفريقين وتفسيرها بأن الهداية قد تتعدى إلى المفعول الثاني بنفسها، وقد تتعدى بواسطة الحرف وقد يفرق بينهما بأن معنى الأول الإيصال إلى المطلوب ولا يكون إلا فعل الله تعالى فلا يسند إلا إليه ومعنى الثاني الدلالة على ما يوصل فيسند إلى القرآن تارة نحو قوله تعالى : يهدي للتي هي أقوم ﴾ [الإسراء : ٩ ] وإلى النبي، أخرى نحو إنك لتهدي إلى صراط مستقيم ﴾ [الشورى : ٥٢] ، والمراد بالهداية في قوله هاهنا وحملوا الهداية إلخ هو المتعدي بنفسه وفي وحواشي المطالع هو المتعدي بالحرف فإنه حمل الهداية في قول المرشد وتيسير مقاصدها والزجر عن طريق الغواية، كما في قوله تعالى: ﴿ وأما ثمود فهديناهم ﴾ [فصلت : ١٧ ]، إذ لا شبهة في امتناع حمله على خلق الهدى فيهم ) والذي يبطله ) أي هذا التأويل ( أمور : الأول : إجماع الأمة على اختلاف الناس فيهما ) أي في التوفيق والهداية فبعضهم موفق مهدي وبعضهم ليس كذلك ( والدعوة عامة ) لجميع الأمة ( لا اختلاف فيها فلا يصح تأويلهما بها ( الثاني : الدعاء بهما نحو اللهم اهدنا الصراط المستقيم ﴾ [الفاتحة: ٦] اللهم وفقنا لما تحب وترضى، والطلب إنما يكون لما ليس بحاصل ( والدعوة ) المذكورة ( حاصلة فلا يتصور طلبها، ( واختلاف الناس ) ليس في الدعوة نفسها بل (في) وجود (الانتفاع بها وعدمه). الثالث : كونه مهدياً وموفقاً من صفات المدح يمدح بهما في المتعارف ( دون كونه مدعواً)، إذ لا يمدح به أصلاً فلا يصح حملها على الدعوة.
(The second) among those matters that they interpret (is tawfiq and hidaya). For the Ash'ari commentator and most of the leading scholars among his followers interpreted tawfiq as creating the ability to obey, and this is appropriate to the linguistic usage because compliance (muwafaqa) is only by obedience, and creating the temporal ability to obey brings about compliance. And Imam al-Haramayn said: tawfiq is creating obedience, not creating ability, since it has no effect. And they interpreted hidaya in its real meaning, i.e., creating guidance (i'tida'), which is faith. And the Mu'tazila interpreted both as invitation (da'wa) to faith and obedience, and clarification of the ways of the aims. His saying: (And they interpreted hidaya in its real meaning) – it was said about this, along with his saying 'and the Mu'tazila interpreted both', that this indicates that hidaya is real in creating guidance by agreement of both groups, and its interpretation as indication (dalala) is an interpretation. So the investigation of whether it remains in its real meaning or is interpreted is a theological discussion. And this contradicts what he mentioned in the commentary on Sharh al-Mutala' that it is real in indicating that which leads to the desired goal, and he argued for it with His saying: 'And as for Thamud, We guided them, but they preferred blindness over guidance' (Qur'an 41:17). The answer is that the purpose here is to clarify the legally intended meaning in most usages of the Lawgiver, while what is mentioned in the glosses on Sharh al-Mutala' is its linguistic or customary meaning, so there is no contradiction. And it can also be repelled by saying that hidaya may be transitive to a second object by itself, and it may be transitive by means of a particle. And one may distinguish between them by saying that the meaning of the first is bringing to the desired goal, and this is only an act of God, so it is not attributed except to Him; and the meaning of the second is indicating that which leads to it, so it is attributed sometimes to the Qur'an, as in His saying 'It guides to what is most upright' (Qur'an 17:9), and sometimes to the Prophet, as in 'You guide to a straight path' (Qur'an 42:52). And the intended meaning of hidaya here in his saying 'and they interpreted hidaya' etc. is the transitive one by itself, while in the glosses on al-Mutala' it is the transitive one by a particle, for he interpreted hidaya in His saying 'As for Thamud, We guided them' (Qur'an 41:17), since there is no doubt that it is impossible to interpret it as creating guidance in them. And that which invalidates this interpretation are matters: First: the consensus of the community on the variation of people regarding them, i.e., regarding tawfiq and hidaya, for some are successful and guided, some are not, while invitation is general for the entire community, there is no variation in it, so it is not correct to interpret them as it. Second: supplication with them, such as 'Guide us to the straight path' (Qur'an 1:6), 'O God, enable us for what You love and are pleased with', and the request is only for what is not present, while invitation is present, so it cannot be imagined to request it, and the variation of people is not in the invitation itself but in the existence of benefit from it or not. Third: being guided and enabled are attributes of praise, one is praised for them conventionally, unlike being invited, since one is not praised for that at all, so it is not correct to interpret them as invitation.
Source pages p. 188

Commentary
This passage defines the Ash'ari understanding of tawfiq (divine enabling) and hidaya (guidance) and refutes the Mu'tazili interpretation that they mean mere invitation. The author first states that Ash'aris interpret tawfiq as creating the ability to obey, and hidaya as creating guidance (faith). Imam al-Haramayn differs slightly, equating tawfiq with creating obedience itself. The Mu'tazila interpret both as invitation. Then a long digression discusses the two senses of hidaya: transitive by itself (bringing to the goal) and transitive by a particle (indicating the way). The author clarifies that in this context hidaya means the former, while in some other works it is used in the latter sense. Then three proofs are given against the Mu'tazili interpretation: (1) Consensus shows people differ in being guided/enabled, whereas invitation is universal and uniform. (2) Prayer for guidance/enabling implies they are not already possessed, while invitation is already present. (3) Being guided/enabled is praiseworthy, while being invited is not. The passage guards against reducing divine guidance to mere human invitation.

Key Terms
توفيق
هداية
خلق القدرة
دعوة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Tawfiq and hidaya are simply God's invitation to faith and obedience.
Correction: The Ash'aris argue they are creative acts: God creates the ability to obey and the faith itself, not just the call.

Checkpoint
According to the Ash'ari commentator, what does tawfiq mean?
A. Creating obedience
B. Creating the ability to obey
C. Inviting to obedience
D. Granting success

Key Takeaway
Tawfiq and hidaya are defined by Ash'aris as God's creation of ability and faith, contrasting with the Mu'tazili view of mere invitation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe that your ability to obey Allah comes from Him alone? Or do you sometimes feel proud of your own efforts, forgetting that even the capacity to act is created by Allah every moment? What would change in your prayer life today if you lived with that awareness?
?
When you feel distant from faith, do you blame yourself as if you could have created guidance on your own, or do you turn to Allah, knowing that He alone is the Creator of hidaya? What does this lesson teach you about where to direct your plea for guidance?
?
If tawfiq is the creation of the ability to obey, how does this affect your view of your own good deeds? Are they truly yours, or are you the recipient of a gift? What would shift in your attitude if you deeply internalized that every act of obedience is a direct creation of Allah?

The Two Senses of Hidaya in the Qur'an
Explains the two senses of hidaya and reconciles apparent contradiction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two senses of hidaya (transitive by itself vs. by particle) and how the author reconciles the apparent contradiction with other works.

Core Idea
Hidaya has two senses: God bringing to the goal, and indicating the way.

A detailed discussion arises when someone objects that hidaya is sometimes defined as 'indication' in other works. The author clarifies that hidaya can be transitive in two ways: one means God actually brings the person to faith (only by God), the other means showing the path (by Qur'an or Prophet). This resolution avoids contradiction.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and response
The passage begins with an objection that hidaya is defined as 'dalala' (indication) elsewhere. Read how the author answers.
The author distinguishes between legal and linguistic meanings.
2
Identify the two types of transitives
Hidaya can be transitive by itself (to bring to goal) or with a particle (to indicate). Note the examples given.
The first is exclusive to God; the second can be from creation.
3
Understand the context
In this passage, hidaya means the first type (bringing to faith). In other works, it might mean the second (indication).
This resolves the apparent conflict.

?

?

?
This lesson shows that both types of hidaya are real and from God. Are you grateful for the guidance of indication (through the Qur'an and Prophet) and the guidance of attainment (when God brings you to a good outcome)? How will you change your dua to ask for both?

Three Proofs Against the Mu'tazili Interpretation
Three proofs refuting the Mu'tazili interpretation of tawfiq and hidaya.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three proofs that refute the Mu'tazili interpretation of tawfiq and hidaya as mere invitation.

Core Idea
Three rational arguments show that divine guidance cannot be reduced to universal invitation.

The author now presents three convincing arguments against the Mu'tazili view that tawfiq and hidaya are just invitation. Each proof draws on consensus, the nature of supplication, and common praise. These arguments highlight the specificity and efficacy of divine action over a general call.

Guided Path
1
Read the three proofs
Read the Arabic passage listing the three proofs. Note that each is introduced with 'al-awwal', 'al-thani', 'al-thalith'.
2
Analyze the first proof: consensus
The first proof argues that people differ in being guided/enabled, but invitation is universal. Therefore guidance cannot be invitation.
This uses the fact of variation.
3
Analyze the second proof: supplication
The second proof points to prayers asking for guidance. We only ask for what we don't have. Invitation is already present, so it cannot be what we ask for.
4
Analyze the third proof: praise
The third proof states that being guided/enabled is praiseworthy, but being invited is not. So they cannot be the same.

(The second) among those matters that they interpret (is tawfiq and hidaya). For the Ash'ari commentator and most of the leading scholars among his followers interpreted tawfiq as creating the ability to obey, and this is appropriate to the linguistic usage because compliance (muwafaqa) is only by obedience, and creating the temporal ability to obey brings about compliance.
