The Inevitability of Evil: Primary and Concomitant Intention
The author answers why evil must exist using the example of cutting a finger to save the body.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why evil is inevitable in a world of many goods and how the concepts of primary and concomitant intention resolve the problem of evil in Islamic theology.

Core Idea
Evil is not willed for itself but necessarily accompanies the greater good.

This lesson addresses a classic theological objection: why does a good God permit evil? The author, building on earlier discussion of rational judgments, shows that evil is not directly willed but arises as a necessary concomitant of a greater good. Using the analogy of cutting a finger to save the body, he explains the principle of 'primary intention' versus 'concomitant intention'. The passage also critiques the philosophers' denial of rational good and evil, affirming the Ash'ari position.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
Read the opening objection: Why was the world not created entirely free of evil? Notice that this question assumes a choice-worthy agent and rational good/evil.
2
Understand the answer
The author replies that it is impossible to strip the world of all evils because many goods necessarily entail a few evils. Good is willed primarily; evil occurs by necessity as a consequence.
Key terms: القصد الأول (primary intention), القصد بالتبع (concomitant intention)
3
Analyze the analogy
The finger-cutting example: a small evil (cutting a finger) is accepted to achieve a great good (saving the body). This illustrates how a wise agent does not abandon a great good for a small evil.
Relate this to the rain example: a few houses may be destroyed, but rain is needed for life.
4
Examine the commentator's critique
The commentator argues that the objection only holds if one assumes choice-worthy agency and rational good/evil—positions the philosophers reject. He then critiques the philosophers' view on power (qudra), deeming their answer weak.
This reinforces the Ash'ari stance that good and evil are determined by divine command, not reason.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

يتأتى إلا مع القول بأن فاعل العالم مختار ومع القول بالحسن والقبح العقليين والفلاسفة لم يقولوا بواحد من هذين الأصلين فلا يتوجه السؤال عليهم حتى يحتاج إلى الجواب، أما أنه لا بد من القول بالاختيار فلانه حسناً صواباً من الله تعالى على ما هو قولنا فلا يتأتى أن يقال : لا يجوز الله تعالى فعل الشر بل يجب أن يكون فاعلاً للخير، فأجاب صاحب المحاكمات الفلاسفة لا يقولون بالأصلين، أما الثاني فلأن الحسن والقبح يطلقان على ملاءمة الطبع ومنافرته وعلى كون الشيء صفة كمال أو صفة نقصان وعلى كون الفعل موجباً للثواب والعقاب والمدح والذم ولا نزاع في الأولين، إنما النزاع في المعنى الأخير، وأما الأول فلما مر في بحث القدرة من قولهم بالاختيار فيتجه أن يقال : إن الله تعالى كامل بالذات خير بالذات فكيف وجد منه الشر والنقصان ويحتاج إلى الجواب المذكور، هذا وأنت قد تحققت في أول بحث القدرة أن قولهم بالقدرة كلام لا تحقيق له وبذلك يظهر ضعف جوابه والله الهادي.
And when it could be said: Why was this world not stripped entirely of evils? He pointed to his answer by saying: 'Then it is not possible to declare this world innocent of evils entirely,' because that which can be completely freed from all evils is the first division, and our discussion concerns many goods to which are attached few evils in comparison to them, and cutting off a thing from what is necessary for it is impossible. Then good occurs by primary intention, entering into al-qada' in an original, essential manner, and evil occurs by necessity, entering into al-qada' by way of concomitance and accident. And he undertook its action, i.e., the action of that which good predominates over, because abandoning much good for the sake of little evil is a great evil. The wise man abandons the rain by which the world lives lest a few houses be destroyed by it, and he does not reflect upon it. A swimmer on land or sea guides you to that: if a person's finger is stung and he knows that if it is cut off the rest of the body will be saved, otherwise corruption will spread to it, he orders its cutting and wills it consequentially to his willing safety from destruction. So the safety of the body is a great good that entails a little evil. Hence, the rational person must choose it; if he refrains from it until he perishes, he is not considered rational, let alone being considered wise as an agent of what is fitting. He said: 'His saying: (And when it could be said, etc.)' contains a discussion, because this objection can only arise together with the assertion that the agent of the world is choice-worthy and with the assertion of rational good and evil. The philosophers did not assert either of these two principles, so the question does not befall them such that an answer is needed. As for the necessity of asserting choice-worthy agency: because if He were necessitated by His essence, then the non-occurrence of His actions from Him would be impossible, whether goods or evils, so it cannot be said: 'Why did He do this?' As for its dependence on rational good and evil: because if one does not say that, then everything is good and correct from God, as is our statement, so it cannot be said: 'It is not permissible for God to do evil; rather He must do good.' The author of al-Muhakamat answered: The philosophers do not assert the two principles. As for the second, because good and evil are applied to agreement and opposition with nature, to being a perfection or a deficiency, and to being a cause of reward and punishment, praise and blame. There is no dispute about the first two; the dispute is only about the last meaning. As for the first, because of what has passed in the discussion of power (qudra) about their assertion of choice, so it may be said: God is perfect in essence, good in essence — how then did evil and deficiency issue from Him? And the aforementioned answer is needed. This, and you have verified in the beginning of the discussion of power that their statement about power is meaningless, and by that the weakness of his answer appears. And God is the Guide.
Source pages p. 199

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and its answer regarding the existence of evil in the world. The objection asks why the world was not entirely free of evil. The answer, attributed to the philosophers but commented upon by the author, is that it is impossible because the world is composed of many goods that necessarily entail some evils; separating the good from its necessary concomitant is impossible. Thus, good is willed primarily, and evil is willed concomitantly. A wise agent does not abandon a great good for a small evil, analogous to a person cutting off a finger to save the whole body. The commentator then analyzes the objection itself, arguing that it only arises if one assumes that the world's agent is choice-worthy and that there is rational good and evil — positions the philosophers do not hold. The commentator elaborates on the necessity of these presuppositions and critiques the philosophers' reply, noting that their own view on power (qudra) is untenable, thus the answer is weak. This analysis follows the author's own argumentative order, first presenting the objection and answer, then discussing the underlying assumptions and ultimately judging the philosophers' response inadequate.

القصد الأول
القصد بالتبع
خير كثير
شر قليل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Evil is directly and primarily willed by God.
Correction: In Ash'ari theology, evil is willed only as a necessary concomitant of a greater good, not for its own sake.

Checkpoint
What example is used to illustrate that a small evil is accepted for a greater good?
A) Cutting off a finger to save the body.
B) Destroying a few houses to build a road.
C) Rain causing minor damage to feed crops.
D) A wise merchant accepting loss.

Key Takeaway
Evil is not intended for itself but necessarily accompanies a greater good, so the wise agent chooses the great good and accepts the unavoidable evil.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a trial or evil in your life, do you truly believe that Allah only wills good for you by primary intention, and that the evil is merely a necessary concomitant? How does this belief affect your patience and gratitude?
?
Is there a 'finger' in your life—a habit, relationship, or comfort—that you are unwilling to cut off to save your iman? What does your hesitation reveal about your trust in Allah's wisdom?
?
If you were the wise agent in the finger-cut example, would you make the cut? Now, apply that same logic to a situation where obeying Allah requires a painful sacrifice: would you still choose the great good of obedience, or would you let the 'body' (your worldly comfort) perish for fear of the minor evil?

The Ash'ari Definitions of Qada' and Qadar
The author states the Ash'ari definitions of qada' and qadar.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari definitions of qada' as God's eternal will and qadar as His specific determination and creative act with measure.

Core Idea
Qada' is eternal will; qadar is determined creation.

This passage presents the foundational Ash'ari definitions of qada' and qadar. These terms are central to the Islamic understanding of divine decree. The author explains that qada' is God's eternal will relating to all things, and qadar is the actual bringing of those things into existence with a specific measure. Understanding these definitions is key to grasping later debates in kalam.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition passage
Read the Arabic and English carefully. Note how qada' and qadar are distinguished.
Qada' relates to will; qadar relates to action.
2
Identify the key terms
Find the terms 'إرادة أزلية' and 'إيجاد على قدر مخصوص' in the text.
3
Understand the implication
Reflect on why this definition implies that God's actions are not necessitated by His essence.
The phrase 'لو كان موجباً لذاته' is crucial.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم أن قضاء الله عند الأشاعرة هو إرادته الأزلية المتعلقة بالأشياء على ما هي عليه فيما لا يزال وقدره إيجاده إياها على قدر مخصوص وتقدير من بمنع أن لو كان موجباً لذاته لكان عدم صدور أفعاله تعالى عنه سبحانه مستحيلاً سواء كانت خيرات أو شرور. قوله : ( هو إرادته الأزلية ) قيل : القضاء في اللغة عبارة عن الفعل مع الإتقان كما دل عليه كلام صاحب الصحاح والإتقان تطبيقه على ما يقتضيه بالحكمة وتعرية له من مظان الخلل، ولهذا وجب الرضاء بالقضاء، فالقول بأنه عبارة عن الإرادة المذكورة يحتاج إلى بيان يدل على وضعه لذلك لغة أو عرفاً أو اصطلاحاً إذ الأصل خلاف الاشتراك والنقل. قوله : ( وقدره إيجاده إياها إلخ قيل : لم يعتبر الإيجاد في وضعه اللغوي والنقل خلاف الأصل ولا دليل عليه كما سلف في القضاء فليس القدرة عبارة عن الإيجاد المذكور بل هو تحديد كل محدود بحده الذي يوجد به .
And know that al-qada' according to the Ash'ariyya is His eternal will relating to things as they are in what will be, and al-qadar is His bringing them into existence with a specific measure and determination from [؟] such that if He were necessitated by His essence, the non-occurrence of His actions from Him would be impossible, whether they be goods or evils. He said: 'It is His eternal will.' It is said: al-qada' in language is an expression for the action together with perfection, as indicated by the words of the author of al-Sihah; and perfection is applying it to what wisdom requires and stripping it of what may be suspected of defect. Therefore, satisfaction with al-qada' is obligatory. So the statement that it is an expression for the aforementioned will requires proof showing its linguistic, customary, or technical usage for that, since the principle is against homonymy and transfer. He said: 'And al-qadar is His bringing them into existence, etc.' It is said: Bringing into existence was not considered in its linguistic usage, and transfer is contrary to the principle, and there is no evidence for it as preceded in al-qada'. So al-qudra is not an expression for the aforementioned bringing into existence; rather it is the determination of each limited thing by the measure by which it comes into existence.
Source pages p. 199

Commentary
This passage provides the Ash'ari definition of qada' and qadar. It begins by stating that qada' (divine decree) is God's eternal will related to all things as they will occur, and qadar (divine determination) is God's bringing those things into existence with a specific measure. The commentator then presents linguistic objections: the word qada' in Arabic originally means 'action with perfection' as per al-Sihah, so claiming it means 'will' requires proof. Similarly, qadar in its linguistic sense does not inherently imply bringing into existence; rather, it means 'determination of a limit'. The commentator argues that the Ash'ari definition relies on a transferred usage without evidence. This passage is a critical gloss questioning the standard Ash'ari terminology from a linguistic perspective, emphasizing that the terms' established meanings differ from their technical usage in kalam.

Key Terms
القضاء
القدر
الإرادة الأزلية
الإيجاد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Qada' and qadar are the same thing and both refer to divine decree.
Correction: Qada' is specifically God's eternal will, while qadar is the actual bringing into existence with measure. They are distinct aspects of the same divine work.

Checkpoint
According to the Ash'ari definition presented here, what is qada'?
A) God's eternal will relating to things.
B) The actual bringing of things into existence.
C) The determination of each thing's measure.
D) The execution of divine justice.

Key Takeaway
Qada' is God's eternal will; qadar is the measured creation of things.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you truly believed that everything that happens is measured by God's specific determination (qadar), how would it change the way you respond to unexpected hardships or failures? Are there areas of your life where you still resist accepting God's decree?
?
The passage teaches that God's actions are not necessitated but chosen. Does your worship reflect a relationship with a God who acts by choice, or do you treat Him as a mechanism of cause and effect? What would change if you lived each moment aware that God's will is free and personal?
?
Considering that qada' is God's eternal will related to all things, does knowing that God's will is eternal and all-encompassing bring you comfort or fear? In what way does your daily life reflect trust in that will?

Linguistic Objections to the Ash'ari Definitions
The author presents linguistic objections to the Ash'ari definitions of qada' and qadar.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the linguistic objections raised against the Ash'ari definitions of qada' and qadar, based on their original Arabic meanings.

Core Idea
Qada' originally means action with perfection, not will; qadar means determination, not creation.

After defining qada' and qadar, the commentator questions these definitions by examining the linguistic roots of the terms. He argues that the words qada' and qadar have established meanings in Arabic that differ from the Ash'ari technical usage. This objection highlights the need for evidence when transferring terms from everyday language to theological jargon.

Guided Path
1
Read the objections
Read the Arabic passage containing the two objections. First, the objection regarding qada', then regarding qadar.
Notice the structure: 'قيل: القضاء في اللغة...' and 'قيل: لم يعتبر الإيجاد...'
2
Identify the classical source
The objection cites the author of al-Sihah (a famous Arabic dictionary) to support the definition of qada'. Recognize the reference.
3
Understand the principle
The commentator applies the rule: 'الأصل خلاف الاشتراك والنقل' – the default is that words are not homonyms nor have they been transferred from their original meaning.
This is a key principle in kalam and usul al-fiqh.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم أن قضاء الله عند الأشاعرة هو إرادته الأزلية المتعلقة بالأشياء على ما هي عليه فيما لا يزال وقدره إيجاده إياها على قدر مخصوص وتقدير من بمنع أن لو كان موجباً لذاته لكان عدم صدور أفعاله تعالى عنه سبحانه مستحيلاً سواء كانت خيرات أو شرور. قوله : ( هو إرادته الأزلية ) قيل : القضاء في اللغة عبارة عن الفعل مع الإتقان كما دل عليه كلام صاحب الصحاح والإتقان تطبيقه على ما يقتضيه بالحكمة وتعرية له من مظان الخلل، ولهذا وجب الرضاء بالقضاء، فالقول بأنه عبارة عن الإرادة المذكورة يحتاج إلى بيان يدل على وضعه لذلك لغة أو عرفاً أو اصطلاحاً إذ الأصل خلاف الاشتراك والنقل. قوله : ( وقدره إيجاده إياها إلخ قيل : لم يعتبر الإيجاد في وضعه اللغوي والنقل خلاف الأصل ولا دليل عليه كما سلف في القضاء فليس القدرة عبارة عن الإيجاد المذكور بل هو تحديد كل محدود بحده الذي يوجد به .
And know that al-qada' according to the Ash'ariyya is His eternal will relating to things as they are in what will be, and al-qadar is His bringing them into existence with a specific measure and determination from [؟] such that if He were necessitated by His essence, the non-occurrence of His actions from Him would be impossible, whether they be goods or evils. He said: 'It is His eternal will.' It is said: al-qada' in language is an expression for the action together with perfection, as indicated by the words of the author of al-Sihah; and perfection is applying it to what wisdom requires and stripping it of what may be suspected of defect. Therefore, satisfaction with al-qada' is obligatory. So the statement that it is an expression for the aforementioned will requires proof showing its linguistic, customary, or technical usage for that, since the principle is against homonymy and transfer. He said: 'And al-qadar is His bringing them into existence, etc.' It is said: Bringing into existence was not considered in its linguistic usage, and transfer is contrary to the principle, and there is no evidence for it as preceded in al-qada'. So al-qudra is not an expression for the aforementioned bringing into existence; rather it is the determination of each limited thing by the measure by which it comes into existence.
Source pages p. 199

Commentary
This passage provides the Ash'ari definition of qada' and qadar. It begins by stating that qada' (divine decree) is God's eternal will related to all things as they will occur, and qadar (divine determination) is God's bringing those things into existence with a specific measure. The commentator then presents linguistic objections: the word qada' in Arabic originally means 'action with perfection' as per al-Sihah, so claiming it means 'will' requires proof. Similarly, qadar in its linguistic sense does not inherently imply bringing into existence; rather, it means 'determination of a limit'. The commentator argues that the Ash'ari definition relies on a transferred usage without evidence. This passage is a critical gloss questioning the standard Ash'ari terminology from a linguistic perspective, emphasizing that the terms' established meanings differ from their technical usage in kalam.

القدر
الإتقان
الاشتراك
النقل

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The technical meanings of qada' and qadar in kalam are the same as their everyday Arabic meanings.
Correction: The commentator shows that the technical definitions differ from the linguistic originals, and that such transfer requires proof.

Checkpoint

A) Eternal will
B) Bringing into existence
C) Action with perfection
D) Determination of measure

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari technical definitions of qada' and qadar differ from their original Arabic meanings, raising the need for justification.

Ash'ari Definition of Qada and Qadr and the Necessity Objection
Definition of qada/qadr and response to necessity objection

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari definition of qada and qadr and how it precludes the philosophical objection based on divine necessity.

Core Idea
Qada and qadr are defined as God's eternal will and specific measure, not necessitation.

This passage begins by defining two key terms in Ash'ari theology: qada and qadr. The author then immediately defends this definition against a challenge from philosophers who argue that if God is necessary in essence, His actions cannot be questioned. Understanding this definition is crucial for grasping the Ash'ari stance on divine decree.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic definition
Carefully read the Arabic text defining qada and qadr. Notice the precise wording: qada is God's eternal will, qadr is bringing into existence with measure.
The definition is concise but foundational.
2
Identify the conditional objection
The author introduces a hypothetical: if God were necessary in essence (wajib li-dhatihi), then His acts would be necessary. This leads to the objection that one cannot ask 'Why did He do this?'
Contrast this with Ash'ari view that God acts by will, not necessity.
3
Understand the conclusion
The Ash'ari definition avoids the objection by grounding acts in will and power, not necessity. Thus the question 'why' is misdirected.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم أن قضاء الله عند الأشاعرة هو إرادته الأزلية المتعلقة بالأشياء على ما هي عليه فيما لا يزال وقدره إيجاده إياها على قدر مخصوص وتقدير من بمنع أن لو كان موجباً لذاته لكان عدم صدور أفعاله تعالى عنه سبحانه مستحيلاً سواء كانت خيرات أو شرور ، فلا يتجه أن يقال : لم فعل هذا وأما توقفه على القول بالحسن والقبح العقليين فلأنه لو لم يقل بذلك كان الكل حسناً صواباً من الله تعالى على ما هو قولنا فلا يتأتى أن يقال : لا يجوز الله تعالى فعل الشر بل يجب أن يكون فاعلاً للخير، فأجاب صاحب المحاكمات الفلاسفة لا يقولون بالأصلين، أما الثاني فلأن الحسن والقبح يطلقان على ملاءمة الطبع ومنافرته وعلى كون الشيء صفة كمال أو صفة نقصان وعلى كون الفعل موجباً للثواب والعقاب والمدح والذم ولا نزاع في الأولين، إنما النزاع في المعنى الأخير، وأما الأول فلما مر في بحث القدرة من قولهم بالاختيار فيتجه أن يقال : إن الله تعالى كامل بالذات خير بالذات فكيف وجد منه الشر والنقصان ويحتاج إلى الجواب المذكور، هذا وأنت قد تحققت في أول بحث القدرة أن قولهم بالقدرة كلام لا تحقيق له وبذلك يظهر ضعف جوابه والله الهادي.
Know that al-qaḍā' of God according to the Ash'arites is His eternal will pertaining to things as they are in what is still to come, and al-qadr is His bringing them into existence according to a specific measure and estimation. [This is] in answer to one who prevents [the objection] that if He were necessary in essence, then the non-occurrence of His acts—exalted is He—from Him would be impossible, whether they are goods or evils. So it is not directed to say: Why did He do this? As for its dependence on affirming rational good and evil (al-ḥusn wa al-qubḥ al-ʿaqliyyān), it is because if one did not say that, then everything would be good and correct from God—exalted—according to our statement, so it cannot be said: God—exalted—does not do evil, rather He must be a doer of good. Then the author of al-Muḥākamāt answered: The philosophers do not uphold the two principles. As for the second, because al-ḥusn and al-qubḥ are used for compatibility with nature and its incompatibility, and for a thing being a quality of perfection or a quality of deficiency, and for an act being a cause for reward and punishment, praise and blame. There is no dispute about the first two; the dispute is only about the last meaning. As for the first [principle], because it passed in the discussion of power (al-qudra) that they say with free choice (al-ikhtiyār), so it is directed to say: God—exalted—is perfect in essence, good in essence, so how can evil and deficiency come from Him? And it needs the mentioned answer. This, and you have verified at the beginning of the discussion of power that their statement about power is speech with no reality, and by that the weakness of his answer appears. And God is the Guide.
Source pages p. 200

Commentary
This passage presents the Ash'ari definition of qada and qadr and then addresses a philosophical objection. The author first defines qada as God's eternal will and qadr as His bringing things into existence with specific measure. He then anticipates an objection: if God were a necessary being (wajib li-dhatihi), then His actions would necessarily occur, so one cannot ask 'Why did He do this?' The objection also relies on the idea of rational good and evil; if one does not accept that God judges by rational standards, then everything from God is good, so the objection that God must be a doer of good does not hold. The author of al-Muhakamat is cited as saying that philosophers do not accept the two principles (al-aslayn) of rational good and evil and free choice. For the second principle (al-husn wa al-qubh al-ʿaqliyyān), the terms have multiple meanings, and the dispute is only about the last meaning (reward and punishment). For the first principle (free choice), since philosophers affirm choice, one can ask why a perfect being would produce evil. The author then remarks that the philosophers' claim about free choice is groundless, as shown earlier in the discussion of power, so their answer is weak.

Key Terms
القضاء
القدر
الموجب بالذات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think qada and qadr mean that God's decrees are necessary and predetermined without will.
Correction: The Ash'ari definition emphasizes that qada is God's eternal will, and qadr is creative act with measure, both stemming from will, not necessity.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, how do the Ash'arites define al-qaḍā'?
A) God's knowledge of the best order.
B) God's eternal will pertaining to things.
C) The act with perfection.
D) The bringing into existence of things.

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari definition of qada and qadr grounds divine decree in God's will and measure, not necessity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This passage teaches that qada is God's eternal will, not blind fate. How does this change the way you understand events in your life that you previously attributed to 'bad luck'? Are you willing to accept them as part of God's will and respond with patience?
?
The objection says that if God were necessary, you could not ask 'why did He do this?' But the Ash'ari definition preserves the possibility of questioning. When you face a calamity, do you find yourself objecting to God's decree or seeking wisdom? What would it mean for your faith to ask 'why' in a spirit of submission rather than protest?
?
The Ash'ari view emphasizes that God's acts are by will and power, not necessity. How does this affect your gratitude for the good you experience? Do you truly thank God as a free act of will, or do you take blessings for granted as if they are necessary?

Objection Based on Rational Good and Evil and the Philosophers' Response
Response to objection based on rational good/evil and free choice

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second part of the philosophical objection concerning rational good and evil, and the Ash'ari rebuttal through the author of al-Muhakamat.

Core Idea
Philosophers reject rational good/evil, but their own stance on free choice leads to a new problem: how can a perfect God produce evil?

The second part of the objection relies on the principle of rational good and evil. The Ash'ari stance that everything from God is good would undercut the objection, but the philosophers do not accept that principle. The author then shows that even on their own grounds, the problem persists, and their answer is weak.

Guided Path
1
Read the second part of the passage
Focus on the text beginning with 'وأما توقفه على القول بالحسن والقبح العقليين' and notice the structure: conditional statement, then the philosophers' answer.
2
Understand the conditional about good and evil
The author argues that if one does not affirm rational good and evil, then all acts from God are good, so the objection that God must do good fails. But the philosophers do not accept this principle.
This shows the objection depends on the principle.
3
Examine the philosophers' answer
The philosophers distinguish three meanings of 'good' and 'evil'. They accept only the first two (compatibility and perfection) and dispute the third (reward/punishment). Additionally, they affirm free choice, which leads to a problem: how can a perfect God produce evil?
The author then dismisses their free choice claim as baseless.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Philosophers on Qada and Qadr
Author presents philosophers' definitions of qada and qadr with refinements from commentaries.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' definitions of qada and qadr, including the concept of 'inaya (providence), and how intellectual substances differ from bodily forms in their relationship to divine decree.

Core Idea
For philosophers, qada is the intellectual blueprint of existence; qadr is its detailed emergence in time.

This passage presents the philosophical view of divine decree (qada) and destiny (qadr), contrasting with the Ash'ari perspective. It introduces key terms like 'inaya (providence) and explains that qada is God's knowledge of the best possible order, while qadr is the actualization of that order through causes. The author then notes a discrepancy with standard philosophical works and provides clarifications from Sharh al-Isharat and al-Muhakamat.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Carefully read the Arabic passage and its English translation to get an overview of the philosophers' definitions of qada and qadr.
Pay attention to the initial definition and the subsequent quotations.
2
Identify the initial definitions
Note that the author first gives a summary: qada is God's knowledge of the best system (called 'inaya), and qadr is the emergence of things into concrete existence according to that decree.
Contrast this with the Ash'ari understanding if known.
3
Examine the scholarly refinement
Read the quotes from Sharh al-Isharat and al-Muhakamat to see how the philosophers refine these concepts. In Sharh al-Isharat, qada is the existence of all existents in the intellectual world collectively, and qadr is their existence in external matter in detail.
Notice the use of 'ibda' (emanation).
4
Distinguish 'inaya from qada
From al-Muhakamat, understand that 'inaya is God's knowledge of the best order with specification, while qada is knowledge of the existence of existents collectively. This distinction is crucial.
The passage emphasises that 'inaya involves a special relation to the most fitting order.
5
Apply to substances and forms
Grasp the final explanation: intellectual substances exist in qada and qadr once (eternally under two aspects), while bodily forms and accidents exist twice (once in eternity in summary, once in time in detail).
This shows the different modes of existence relative to divine decree.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
معين في ذواتها وأحوالها، وأما عند الفلاسفة فالقضاء عبارة عن علمه بما ينبغي أن يكون عليه الوجود حتى يكون على أحسن النظام وأكمل الانتظام، وهو المسمى عندهم بالعناية التي هي مبدأ لفيضان الموجودات من حيث جملتها على أحسن الوجوه وأكملها، والقدر عبارة عن خروجها إلى الوجود العيني بأسبابها على الوجه الذي تقرر في القضاء [...] قوله : ( وأما عند الفلاسفة فالقضاء عبارة إلخ ) هذا يخالف ما في مشاهير الكتب الحكمية قال الحكيم المحقق في شرح الإشارات: اعلم أن القضاء عبارة عن وجود جميع الموجودات في العالم العقلي مجتمعة على سبيل الإبداع والقدر عبارة عن وجودها في موادها الخارجية مفصلة واحداً بعد واحد . وقال في المحاكمات: أما العناية فهو علم الله تعالى بالموجودات على أحسن النظام والترتيب على ما يجب أن يكون لكل موجود من الآلات بحسب ترتب الكمالات المطلوبة منه عليها والفرق بينها وبين القضاء أن في مفهوم العناية تخصيصاً وهو تعلق العلم بالوجه الأصلح والنظام الأليق بخلاف القضاء فإنه العلم بوجود الموجودات جملة . هذا واعلم أن الجواهر العقلية موجودة في القضاء والقدر مرة واحدة إذ لا وجود لها إلا في الأزل ولكن باعتبارين وأما الصور والأعراض الجسمانية فهي موجودة فيها مرتين مرة في الأزل مجملة ومرة فيما لا يزال مفصلة .
Appointed in their essences and states. As for the philosophers, al-qaḍā' is an expression for His knowledge of how existence ought to be so that it is upon the best system and most perfect order, and it is called by them al-ʿināya (providence) which is the principle for the emanation of existents from their totality in the best and most complete ways. And al-qadr is an expression for their emergence into concrete existence with their causes in the manner determined in al-qaḍā'. [...] His saying: 'And as for the philosophers, al-qaḍā' is an expression, etc.' This contradicts what is in the well-known philosophical books. The veracious philosopher said in Sharḥ al-Ishārāt: Know that al-qaḍā' is an expression for the existence of all existents in the intellectual world collectively in the mode of emanation (ibdāʿ), and al-qadr is an expression for their existence in their external matters in detail one after another. And he said in al-Muḥākamāt: As for al-ʿināya, it is God's knowledge of existents in the best system and arrangement, according to what every existent must have of instruments in accordance with the order of perfections required from it. The difference between it and al-qaḍā' is that the concept of al-ʿināya involves specification—it is the attachment of knowledge to the most appropriate aspect and the most fitting order—contrary to al-qaḍā', for it is knowledge of the existence of existents collectively. Know that the intellectual substances exist in al-qaḍā' and al-qadr once, since they have no existence except in eternity but under two aspects. As for bodily forms and accidents, they exist in them twice: once in eternity in summary, and once in time in detail.
Source pages p. 201

Commentary
This passage defines qada and qadr according to the philosophers and then contrasts it with the view of the well-known philosophical books. The author first gives a summary: qada is God's knowledge of the best possible system, called 'inaya, and qadr is the emergence of things into concrete existence according to that decree. Then he notes that this contradicts what is found in standard philosophical works. He quotes from Sharh al-Isharat: qada is the existence of all existents in the intellectual world collectively (by way of ibda'), and qadr is their existence in external matter in detail. Then from al-Muhakamat: 'inaya is God's knowledge of the best order, with specification of what is most fitting, whereas qada is knowledge of existence collectively. Finally, he explains that intellectual substances exist in qada and qadr once (eternally under two aspects), while bodily forms and accidents exist twice: in eternal summary and in temporal detail.

Key Terms
القضاء

Misconception & Correction

Correction: The passage clarifies that 'inaya is a more specific knowledge involving the best order, whereas qada is knowledge of the existence of all existents collectively.

According to the quotation from Sharḥ al-Ishārāt, what is al-qaḍā'?
A) God's knowledge of the best system.
B) The existence of all existents in the intellectual world collectively.
C) The emergence of things into concrete existence.
D) God's eternal will.

Key Takeaway
For philosophers, qada is the eternal intellectual blueprint of all existents, and qadr is their temporal detailed emergence; intellectual substances exist only once in that blueprint, while bodily forms exist twice.

?
If intellectual substances exist only once in God's knowledge, while bodily forms exist both in summary and in detail, what does that say about the permanence of your soul compared to your physical actions? How should that affect your priorities today?

Philosophers' Definitions of Qada and Qadar
Definitions and corrections of philosophers' qada, qadar, and 'inaya.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' concepts of qada and qadar, including their notion of 'inaya (providence), and how they differ from the Ash'ari understanding.

Core Idea
For philosophers, qada is the collective blueprint of existence in the divine intellect, and qadar is its detailed unfolding in the physical world.

Al-Jurjani presents the philosophers' view on qada and qadar, which are central to their emanationist cosmology. He explains that qada is God's knowledge of the best possible order, called 'inaya, and qadar is the actual coming-into-being of things through their causes. This passage also includes a correction from philosophical authorities that refines these definitions.

Guided Path
1
Read the initial definition
Begin by reading the first part of the passage where Al-Jurjani defines qada as God's knowledge of the best system and qadar as the emergence of things into existence through their causes. Note the term 'inaya (providence).
This definition is later refined by the commentator.
2
Understand the correction
The commentator notes that this definition contradicts standard philosophical works. Pay attention to the corrected definitions from Sharh al-Isharat and al-Muhakamat.
The correction emphasizes qada as collective existence and qadar as detailed existence.
3
Distinguish 'inaya from qada
From al-Muhakamat, understand that 'inaya is God's knowledge with specification of the most beneficial order, while qada is simply knowledge of existence collectively.
4
Apply to different beings
The passage explains that intellectual substances exist in qada and qadar once (in pre-eternity), while physical forms exist twice: once summarized in pre-eternity and once in detail in time.
This illustrates how qada and qadar apply across different realms.
5
Review key terms
Revisit the terms qada', qadar, 'inaya, and their definitions in this philosophical context. Ensure you can distinguish between the initial and corrected definitions.
This solidifies the lesson's core terminology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( وأما عند الفلاسفة فالقضاء عبارة عن علمه بما ينبغي أن يكون عليه الوجود حتى يكون على أحسن النظام وأكمل الانتظام، وهو المسمى عندهم بالعناية التي هي مبدأ لفيضان الموجودات من حيث جملتها على أحسن الوجوه وأكملها، والقدر عبارة عن خروجها إلى الوجود العيني بأسبابها على الوجه الذي تقرر في القضاء والمعتزلة ينكرون القضاء والقدر في الأفعال الاختيارية الصادرة العباد ويثبتون علمه تعالى بهذه الأفعال ولا يسندون وجودها إلى ذلك العلم بل إلى اختيار العباد وقدرتهم. قوله : ( وأما عند الفلاسفة فالقضاء عبارة إلخ ) هذا يخالف ما في مشاهير الكتب الحكمية قال الحكيم المحقق في شرح الإشارات: اعلم أن القضاء عبارة عن وجود جميع الموجودات في العالم العقلي مجتمعة على سبيل الإبداع والقدر عبارة عن وجودها في موادها الخارجية مفصلة واحداً بعد واحد . وقال في المحاكمات: أما العناية فهو علم الله تعالى بالموجودات على أحسن النظام والترتيب على ما يجب أن يكون لكل موجود من الآلات بحسب ترتب الكمالات المطلوبة منه عليها والفرق بينها وبين القضاء أن في مفهوم العناية تخصيصاً وهو تعلق العلم بالوجه الأصلح والنظام الأليق بخلاف القضاء فإنه العلم بوجود الموجودات جملة . هذا واعلم أن الجواهر العقلية موجودة في القضاء والقدر مرة واحدة إذ لا وجود لها إلا في الأزل ولكن باعتبارين وأما الصور والأعراض الجسمانية فهي موجودة فيها مرتين مرة في الأزل مجملة ومرة فيما لا يزال مفصلة . قوله : ( والمعتزلة ينكرون القضاء والقدر إلخ) أما إنكارهم القدر فظاهر لإسنادهم خلق أفعال العباد إليهم ، وأما إنكارهم القضاء فيها فإما لأنهم لم يقولوا بإرادة الله تعالى لأفعالهم بالذات بل قالوا: إنه تعالى أراد أن يفعلوها بقدرهم ورغباتهم وإما لأنهم أنكروا الإرادة في فعل السيئة والمباح .
His saying: 'As for the philosophers, al-qada' is an expression for His knowledge of what existence ought to be so that it may be upon the best system and most complete order, and it is what they call al-'inaya (providence) which is the principle for the emanation of existents in their totality in the best and most complete ways; and al-qadar is an expression for their emergence into concrete existence through their causes in the manner determined in al-qada'. And the Mu'tazila deny al-qada' and al-qadar regarding the voluntary acts emanating from servants, and they affirm His knowledge of these acts but do not attribute their existence to that knowledge, rather to the servants' choice and power. His saying: 'As for the philosophers, al-qada' is an expression...' This contradicts what is in well-known philosophical books. The verified philosopher said in Sharh al-Isharat: Know that al-qada' is an expression for the existence of all existents in the intellectual world collectively on the way of origination, and al-qadar is an expression for their existence in their external matters one by one. And he said in al-Muhakamat: As for al-'inaya, it is Allah's knowledge of existing things upon the best system and arrangement according to what each existent must have of instruments in terms of the arrangement of the perfections sought from it; and the difference between it and al-qada' is that the concept of al-'inaya includes specification, which is the attachment of knowledge to the most beneficial aspect and the most appropriate order, unlike al-qada' which is knowledge of the existence of existents collectively. This: know that intellectual substances exist in al-qada' and al-qadar once, since they have no existence except in pre-eternity, but under two aspects; as for physical forms and bodily accidents, they exist in them twice: once in pre-eternity summarily and once in perpetuity in detail. His saying: 'And the Mu'tazila deny al-qada' and al-qadar' etc. As for their denial of al-qadar, it is apparent due to their attribution of the creation of servants' acts to themselves. As for their denial of al-qada' in them, it is either because they did not affirm Allah's will for their acts in essence, but said that He willed that they do them by their power and desires, or because they denied will in the act of evil and permissible.
Source pages p. 201

Commentary
This passage explains the philosophical concept of qada and qadar, contrasting it with the Ash'ari view, and then discusses the Mu'tazila position of denying qada and qadar for voluntary acts. The commentator provides a detailed analysis of the definitions from philosophical authorities like the author of Sharh al-Isharat and al-Muhakamat, clarifying that for philosophers, qada is the collective existence of all things in the intellectual world, and qadar is their detailed entry into matter. The Mu'tazila deny qada and qadar for human acts because they believe humans create their own actions and deny that Allah's will directly determines sinful or permissible acts.

Misconception: Philosophers' qada and qadar are identical to the Ash'ari understanding of divine decree.
Correction: Philosophers interpret qada as collective existence in the intellect and qadar as detailed emergence, whereas Ash'aris see qada as God's eternal decree encompassing all things.

Key Takeaway
The philosophers view qada as the collective blueprint of all existence in the divine intellect and qadar as the gradual realization of that blueprint in the physical world.

?

?
If everything exists first intellectually in qada and then unfolds in qadar, does that challenge you to see your daily struggles not as random but as part of a pre-eternal design? How can you live today with the awareness that your current moment is the detailed expression of a divine plan?
?
The passage distinguishes between intellectual substances that exist only once and physical things that exist twice. Are you living as if your soul is merely physical, or are you cultivating the intellectual, eternal aspect of your being that aligns with qada? What step will you take today to prioritize your spiritual and intellectual substance over fleeting physical concerns?

Mu'tazila Rejection of Qada and Qadar for Human Acts
Explanation of Mu'tazila denial of qada and qadar for voluntary acts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Mu'tazila deny qada and qadar for voluntary human acts and how they reconcile God's knowledge with human free will.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazila affirm God's knowledge of actions but deny that His will or decree determines human choices.

This passage shifts to the Mu'tazila position, who reject qada and qadar for human voluntary acts. They assert that humans create their own actions and that God's knowledge does not necessitate the acts; rather, acts come about through human choice and power. Al-Jurjani explains that they deny qadar because they attribute creation of acts to humans, and they deny qada either because they do not affirm God's will for these acts essentially or because they deny His will for evil and neutral acts.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on Mu'tazila
Read the Arabic and translation carefully, noting the key points: they deny qada and qadar for voluntary acts, affirm God's knowledge, and attribute acts to human choice and power.
The passage is concise but dense.
2
Understand the denial of qadar
The commentator explains that denying qadar is obvious from their belief that humans create their own acts. Reflect on how this follows from their principles.
Qadar here refers to the predetermination of acts.
3
Understand the denial of qada
The passage gives two possible interpretations for their denial of qada: either they deny that God wills human acts essentially, or they deny His will specifically for evil and permissible acts.
This shows internal differences among Mu'tazila.
4
Compare with Ash'ari view
Contrast this with the Ash'ari view that God creates all acts and determines them. Consider the implications for divine justice and human responsibility.

Classification of Objections: Refutation and Sophistry
Commentator clarifies two types of objections.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the commentator distinguishes between an objection that is a direct refutation and one that leads to sophistry.

Core Idea
Not every objection is a refutation; some lead to sophistry.

The commentator provides a marginal note clarifying two types of objections against a kalam argument. One is a genuine refutation that negates divine power and moral categories, the other is only 'in its ruling' because it leads to sophistry. This distinction is crucial for precise theological reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the translation. Let the commentator's own words come through.
2
Identify the two categories
Notice the terms 'naqd' and 'fi hukmihi'. Understand what each one entails according to the commentator.
The first negates divine power; the second leads to sophistry.
3
Ask why it matters
Reflect on why distinguishing these two types helps maintain sound theological argumentation.
This connects to the learning objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (إما نقض أو في (حكمه كونه نقضاً بالنظر إلى لزوم نفي قدرة الله تعالى والحسن والقبح الشرعيين وكونه في حكمه باعتبار لزوم السفسطة.
قوله : (هو إرادته وقدرته فيه نظر لأن هذا الكلام على تقدير تسليم احتياج الفعل إلى الداعي كما يدل عليه سياق كلام الشارح والمرجح الذي ذكره أعني الإرادة والقدرة ليس بداع فالصواب أن يقال : هو علم بالنفع فتدبر.
قوله : ( والمتعلقتان بالفعل في وقت مخصوص) الظاهر أن مراده بالوقت المخصوص هو وقت وجود الفعل وحينئذ لا وجه للقول بأن المرجح قديم لأن التعلق معتبر في المرجح فيكون المرجح متجدد. اللهم إلا أن يقال: إن المراد بالوقت المخصوص الأزل بأن يتعلقا في الأزل بوجود المقدور فيما لا يزال ولا حاجة إلى تعلق آخر حادث .
His saying: 'either a refutation or in its ruling' meaning it is a refutation in view of the necessary negation of God's power and the two legal judgments, and it is in its ruling in view of the necessary sophistry.
His saying: 'it is His will and power' there is a consideration in this, because this statement assumes the need of the act for a motive as indicated by the context of the commentator's words, and the preponderator he mentioned, namely will and power, is not a motive. So the correct thing is to say: it is knowledge of benefit, so reflect.
His saying: 'and the two are related to the act in a specific time' apparently his intended meaning by 'specific time' is the time of the act's existence, and then there is no basis for saying the preponderator is eternal, because the relation is considered in the preponderator, so the preponderator would be renewed. Unless it is said that what is meant by 'specific time' is pre-eternity, such that they relate in pre-eternity to the existence of the predetermined in what will not cease, and there is no need for another originated relation.
Source pages p. 207

Commentary
This passage contains three marginal notes from the commentator on phrases in the main text. The first note (on 'either a refutation or in its ruling') explains that the term 'refutation' (naqd) refers to the necessary negation of God's power and the legal good/evil, while 'in its ruling' refers to the necessary sophistry (safsata). The second note (on 'it is His will and power') offers a critical observation: the author's statement assumes the act's need for a motive (da'iyy), but will and power are not motives; the correct preponderator is knowledge of benefit ('ilm bi al-naf'). The commentator advises reflection. The third note (on 'related to the act in a specific time') addresses a potential contradiction: if the specific time means the time of the act's existence, then the preponderator would be temporally renewed, contradicting its eternality. The commentator resolves this by suggesting that 'specific time' might mean pre-eternity (al-azal), where God's will and power relate from pre-eternity to the future existence of the predetermined, requiring no new relation.

Key Terms
نقض
سفسطة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the commentator, what does the term 'naqd' (refutation) necessarily entail?
A) It leads to sophistry.
B) It negates God's power and the two legal judgments.
C) It is always invalid.
D) It requires a motive.

Key Takeaway
Classifying objections as either direct refutations or sophistry-bound helps maintain sound theological argumentation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a time you objected to a religious ruling or belief. Was your objection a genuine refutation that could be answered, or did it ultimately lead to sophistry—confusion without resolution? How does the classification in this passage help you examine your own intentions?
?
When you engage in theological discussions, do you seek to refute falsehood or to win arguments? The passage shows that some objections are sophistry. Which camp do your own objections typically fall into? Be honest.

The True Preponderator: Knowledge of Benefit
Commentator corrects the identity of the preponderator.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the commentator rejects will and power as the preponderator and instead identifies knowledge of benefit.

Core Idea
Will and power are not motives; knowledge of benefit is the true preponderator.

In this marginal note, the commentator critically examines the claim that God's will and power serve as the preponderator (murajjih) for His actions. He argues that a motive (da'iyy) is needed, and that will and power do not qualify. Instead, the correct preponderator is knowledge of benefit ('ilm bi al-naf'). This correction is essential for understanding divine action in kalam.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the passage to see the commentator's critique. Note the phrase 'فيه نظر' (there is consideration).
2
Identify the key claim and its problem
The main text said the preponderator is will and power. The commentator says this assumes the act needs a motive, but will and power are not motives.
A motive is something that inclines the agent, not the mere capacity.
3
Understand the correction
The commentator proposes 'knowledge of benefit' as the correct preponderator. Reflect on why knowledge of benefit can serve as a motive.
This connects to kalam discussions of divine wisdom.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (إما نقض أو في (حكمه كونه نقضاً بالنظر إلى لزوم نفي قدرة الله تعالى والحسن والقبح الشرعيين وكونه في حكمه باعتبار لزوم السفسطة.
قوله : (هو إرادته وقدرته فيه نظر لأن هذا الكلام على تقدير تسليم احتياج الفعل إلى الداعي كما يدل عليه سياق كلام الشارح والمرجح الذي ذكره أعني الإرادة والقدرة ليس بداع فالصواب أن يقال : هو علم بالنفع فتدبر.
قوله : ( والمتعلقتان بالفعل في وقت مخصوص) الظاهر أن مراده بالوقت المخصوص هو وقت وجود الفعل وحينئذ لا وجه للقول بأن المرجح قديم لأن التعلق معتبر في المرجح فيكون المرجح متجدد. اللهم إلا أن يقال: إن المراد بالوقت المخصوص الأزل بأن يتعلقا في الأزل بوجود المقدور فيما لا يزال ولا حاجة إلى تعلق آخر حادث .
His saying: 'either a refutation or in its ruling' meaning it is a refutation in view of the necessary negation of God's power and the two legal judgments, and it is in its ruling in view of the necessary sophistry.
His saying: 'it is His will and power' there is a consideration in this, because this statement assumes the need of the act for a motive as indicated by the context of the commentator's words, and the preponderator he mentioned, namely will and power, is not a motive. So the correct thing is to say: it is knowledge of benefit, so reflect.
His saying: 'and the two are related to the act in a specific time' apparently his intended meaning by 'specific time' is the time of the act's existence, and then there is no basis for saying the preponderator is eternal, because the relation is considered in the preponderator, so the preponderator would be renewed. Unless it is said that what is meant by 'specific time' is pre-eternity, such that they relate in pre-eternity to the existence of the predetermined in what will not cease, and there is no need for another originated relation.
Source pages p. 207

Commentary
This passage contains three marginal notes from the commentator on phrases in the main text. The first note (on 'either a refutation or in its ruling') explains that the term 'refutation' (naqd) refers to the necessary negation of God's power and the legal good/evil, while 'in its ruling' refers to the necessary sophistry (safsata). The second note (on 'it is His will and power') offers a critical observation: the author's statement assumes the act's need for a motive (da'iyy), but will and power are not motives; the correct preponderator is knowledge of benefit ('ilm bi al-naf'). The commentator advises reflection. The third note (on 'related to the act in a specific time') addresses a potential contradiction: if the specific time means the time of the act's existence, then the preponderator would be temporally renewed, contradicting its eternality. The commentator resolves this by suggesting that 'specific time' might mean pre-eternity (al-azal), where God's will and power relate from pre-eternity to the future existence of the predetermined, requiring no new relation.

Key Terms
داع
قديم
تعلق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That God's will and power can serve as the preponderator for His actions without a motive.
Correction: The commentator clarifies that will and power are not motives; the actual preponderator is knowledge of benefit, which serves as the motive.

Checkpoint
What does the commentator propose as the correct preponderator for God's actions?
A) Will and power.
B) Knowledge of benefit.
C) The inherent goodness of the act.
D) The act's being the best.

Key Takeaway
For divine action, the true preponderator is knowledge of benefit, not will and power, because it serves as the motive.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you attribute your own actions to your will and power, do you neglect the deeper motive of seeking benefit that Allah has placed? How can you train yourself to align your intentions with the benefit that Allah knows, rather than just your desires?
?
The commentator ends with 'so reflect.' What specific reflection does this correction demand from you regarding your understanding of Allah's attributes? If you truly believed that every divine action is motivated by knowledge of benefit, how would that change your trust in His decree?
?
What prevents you from deeply pondering the attributes of Allah? Are you content with superficial understanding? Commit today to spending ten minutes reflecting on one divine attribute each day, starting with Allah's knowledge as the true motive for His actions.

The Eternality of the Preponderator and Temporal Acts
Commentator resolves a potential contradiction about the preponderator's eternality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the commentator resolves the apparent conflict between an eternal preponderator and its relation to temporal acts.

Core Idea
The relation of will and power to acts is eternal if 'specific time' means pre-eternity.

The third marginal note addresses a subtle issue: if God's will and power relate to an act at a specific time, then that relation would be temporal, implying the preponderator is not eternal. The commentator offers a resolution by interpreting 'specific time' as pre-eternity (al-azal), so the relation is eternal and no new relation is needed. This nuance defends the classical Ash'ari position.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the passage to see the commentator's reasoning. Note the problem and the proposed resolution.
2
Identify the problem
If 'specific time' means the time of the act's existence, then the relation would be temporal, making the preponderator non-eternal.
This contradicts the preponderator's eternality.
3
Understand the resolution
The commentator suggests 'specific time' might mean pre-eternity, so the relation is established from eternity to the future act, needing no new relation.
This preserves the preponderator's eternality.

Source Text

?

Can you surrender your impatience and accept that His eternal plan is already at work, requiring no new 'divine decision'? Make a specific commitment to trust His timing today, without needing to see evidence of His action.

The Compound Consensus That Allah Does No Evil
States the compound consensus and explains Ash'ari and Mu'tazili bases.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the compound consensus on Allah not doing evil and the differing bases of Ash'aris and Mu'tazilis.

Core Idea
All Muslims agree Allah does no evil, but they disagree on why.

This lesson introduces a foundational agreement among Muslims: Allah does not commit evil. But beneath this agreement lies a deep theological difference between Ash'aris and Mu'tazilis about how we know what is good and evil. Let's explore the consensus and its two rational foundations.

Guided Path
1
Read the consensus statement
Start by reading the opening lines where the compound consensus is stated. Notice the three elements: Allah does no evil, does not neglect an obligation, and nothing is obligatory upon Him.
The term 'compound consensus' means agreement on the ruling but not on the basis.
2
Identify the two schools' positions
Look for how Ash'aris and Mu'tazilis each explain why Allah does no evil. Ash'aris say no act is evil from Him; Mu'tazilis say He leaves evil and does good.
3
Connect to the principle of moral evaluation
Note that the difference traces back to al-tahsin wa al-taqbih (moral evaluation by reason). Ash'aris deny reason can judge good and evil; Mu'tazilis affirm it.
This principle is the root of many later arguments.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقصد السادس : إن الله لا يفعل القبيح

اعلم أن الأمة قد أجمعت إجماعاً مركباً على أن الله لا يفعل القبيح ولا يترك واجباً ولا يجب عليه شيء فلا يتصور فعل قبيح ولا ترك واجب، فالأشاعرة من جهة أنه لا قبيح منه ( عليه ) ولا ترك واجب ( وأما المعتزلة فمن جهة أن ما هو قبيح منه يتركه وما يجب يفعله (وهذا الخلاف في مبنى الحكم المتفق عليه ) فرع المسألة المتقدم أعني قاعدة التحسين والتقبيح ( إذ لا حاكم بقبح القبيح منه ووجوب الواجب عليه إلا العقل).

فإن قيل : الكفر والظلم والمعاصي كلها قبائح وقد خلقها الله تعالى . قلت : نعم إلا أن خلق القبيح ليس بقبيح فهو موجد للقبيح لا فاعل له، فإن قيل : فلا يفعل الحسن أيضاً، لأنه لا حكم عليه أمراً كما لا حكم عليه نهياً والإجماع على خلافه . قلنا : قد ورد الشرع بالثناء في أفعاله فكانت حسنة لكونها متعلقة للمدح والثناء عند الله تعالى وأما إذا اكتفى في الحسن بعدم استحقاق الذم في حكم الله تعالى فالأمر أظهر .

فمن جعله حاكماً بالحسن والقبح قال بقبح بعض الأفعال منه ووجوب بعضها عليه (و) نحن ( قد أبطلنا حكمه وبينا فيما تقدم ( أنه تعالى الحاكم فيحكم ما يريد ) ويفعل ما يشاء، لا وجوب عليه كما لا وجوب عنه ولا استقباح له
The sixth aim: Allah does not do evil.
Know that the community has concurred in a compound consensus (ijma' murakkab) that Allah does not do evil, does not neglect an obligatory act, and nothing is obligatory upon Him; hence no evil act or neglect of an obligatory act can be conceived. For the Ash'aris, it is because no act is evil from Him, nor does He neglect an obligation. As for the Mu'tazilis, they hold that what is evil from Him He leaves, and what is obligatory He does. This difference is in the basis of the agreed-upon ruling, which is a branch of the preceding issue, namely the principle of moral evaluation (al-tahsin wa al-taqbih), since there is no judge of the evil of an evil act from Him or the obligation of an obligatory act upon Him except reason.
If it is said: Unbelief, injustice, and sins are all evils, and Allah has created them. We say: Yes, but creating an evil act is not itself evil; He is the originator of the evil act, not a doer of it. If it is said: Then He also does not do good, since He is not commanded as He is not forbidden, but consensus is contrary to that. We say: The Law has come with praise for His acts, so they are good because they are objects of praise and commendation with Allah. As for when one is satisfied with good being the lack of liability to blame in Allah's judgment, the matter is even clearer.
He who makes reason a judge of good and evil holds that some acts from Him are evil and some are obligatory upon Him. But we have invalidated its judgment and shown earlier that He is the judge, judging what He wills and doing what He wills. There is no obligation upon Him, just as there is no obligation from Him, and no act is deemed evil from Him.
Source pages p. 216

Commentary
This passage states the compound consensus (ijma' murakkab) that Allah does no evil. It explains the differing bases: Ash'aris deny any evil from Allah because He is the creator of all acts and evil is not attributable to Him; Mu'tazilis hold that evil is known by reason and Allah avoids it. Then it addresses an objection: how can created evils like unbelief be reconciled? The answer distinguishes between creating evil (not evil) and doing evil. It also handles an objection about the good acts, explaining legal praise for Allah's acts. Finally, it concludes by reaffirming the Ash'ari position that reason does not judge good and evil; Allah is the sole judge.

Key Terms
الإجماع المركب
القبيح
الواجب
التحسين والتقبيح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The consensus means Allah refrains from evil as a moral agent, like humans avoiding wrongdoing.
Correction: The consensus is about the ruling, not the basis. Ash'aris deny evil is even attributable to Allah, while Mu'tazilis affirm He avoids it by choice.

Checkpoint
What is the basis of the Ash'ari position that Allah does no evil?
A) Reason judges evil and Allah avoids it
B) No act from Allah is evil
C) Allah is commanded not to do evil
D) The community forced Him to avoid evil

Key Takeaway
All Muslims agree Allah does no evil, but Ash'aris and Mu'tazilis differ on whether reason can judge evil.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If all Muslims agree that Allah does no evil, but disagree on whether reason can determine that, which camp do you find yourself leaning toward? What does that say about how you decide right from wrong in your own life?
?
Think of a moral conviction you hold strongly. Is it based on revelation or on your own reasoning? How would your life change if you had to derive all morality solely from reason?
?
The compound consensus shows that agreement on a ruling can mask deep disagreement on foundations. Is there a belief you hold that you and a friend agree on, but for completely different reasons? How does that affect the strength of your shared conviction?

Reconciling Created Evils with Allah's Acts
Addresses objections about created evils and defends Ash'ari position.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ash'ari theologians respond to the objection that Allah creates evil acts like unbelief and injustice.

Core Idea
Creating evil is not the same as doing evil.

If Allah does no evil, why does He create unbelief, injustice, and sin? This objection has challenged theologians for centuries. In this lesson, we follow the author's response, which distinguishes between creating an evil act and being the doer of it. We also see how the Ash'ari position handles the parallel objection about good acts.

Guided Path
1
Read the first objection and answer
Start with the objection that Allah created evils like kufr and injustice. Then read the answer: creating an evil act is not itself evil; He is the originator, not the doer.
This distinction is key to Ash'ari theology.
2
Examine the second objection and answer
The second objection asks: if Allah neither does evil nor good, then He does no good either. The answer invokes legal praise from revelation: Allah's acts are good because they are praised in the Law.
This shows the role of revelation in determining good.
3
Review the final reaffirmation
The passage concludes by restating the Ash'ari position: reason cannot judge good and evil; Allah alone judges, and nothing is obligatory or evil from Him.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Refuting the Obligation of Divine Grace
Refutes the Mu'tazili claim that Allah must provide grace (lutf) for obedience.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazili claim that Allah must provide grace (lutf) and why the Ash'ari refutation finds it self-defeating.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazili proof for lutf collapses under its own logic.

The Mu'tazili argue that Allah must send prophets and other aids to obedience, otherwise His wisdom would be violated. But as this passage shows, their own proof would require even more extensive grace that they do not actually believe is obligatory. This lesson unpacks the definition and the refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Let the author's argument come through before analyzing.
Notice the two-part structure: first the refutation via counterexample, then the critique of the definition.
2
Understand the definition of lutf
The Mu'tazili define lutf as an act that draws closer to obedience without compulsion, like sending prophets. They claim it is rationally obligatory.
This definition is key to the refutation.
3
Follow the counterexample
The author argues that if lutf were obligatory, then sending a prophet in every age and infallible guide in every region would be even more of a grace, yet the Mu'tazilis do not require that. Thus the proof is inconsistent.
This is a classic reductio ad absurdum.
4
Examine the definition of rational obligation
The passage then clarifies that the Mu'tazili define rational obligation as that whose omission deserves blame or violates wisdom. The Ash'ari rebuts both.
Pay attention to the two rebuttals.
5
Reflect on the two rebuttals
First, Allah is the absolute owner, so He cannot be blamed for disposing of His property. Second, His omissions always have countless benefits, so wisdom is not violated; moreover, necessity would imply inability, contradicting free will.
This reinforces divine sovereignty and freedom.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأول: اللطف وفسروه بأنه الفعل الذي يقرب العبد إلى الطاعة ويبعده عن المعصية ولا ينتهي إلى حد الإلجاء كبعثة الأنبياء، فإنا نعلم بالضرورة أن الناس معها أقرب إلى الطاعة وأبعد عن المعصية. فيقال لهم : هذا الدليل الذي تمسكتم به في وجوب اللطف ينتقض بأمور لا تحصى، فإنا نعلم أنه لو كان في كل عصر نبي وفي كل بلد معصوم يأمر بالمعروف وينهى عن المنكر وكان حكام الأطراف مجتهدين متفقين لكان لطفاً، وأنتم لا توجبونه على الله تعالى بل نجزم بعدمه فلا يكون واجباً عليه.

قوله : ( أوجبوا عليه تعالى) فسروا الوجوب العقلي بما يستحق تاركه الذم عند العقل وبما يلزم صدوره لا خلال تركه بالحكمة ورد على الأول بأنه المالك مطلقاً فلا يستحق الذم بالتصرف في ملكه وعلى الثاني بأن له تعالى في كل ترك مصالح لا تحصى فلا يخل بالحكمة على أنه لا معنى للزوم إلا عدم التمكن من الترك وهو ينافي الاختيار، فتأمل.
First: grace (lutf). They define it as an act that draws the servant closer to obedience and distances him from disobedience, without reaching the point of compulsion, such as the sending of prophets. For we know by necessity that people with them are closer to obedience and farther from disobedience. It is said to them: This proof that you have relied upon for the obligation of grace is refuted by countless things. For we know that if there were in every age a prophet and in every region an infallible one commanding good and forbidding evil, and the rulers of the regions were all diligent and agreed, that would be a grace, yet you do not require it of Allah—rather, we are certain of its non-existence. Hence it is not obligatory upon Him.
His saying: (They obligated upon Him) They interpreted rational obligation (al-wujub al-'aqli) as that whose neglect deserves blame in reason, and as that whose occurrence is necessary such that its omission would violate wisdom. It is responded to the first that He is the absolute owner, so He does not deserve blame for disposing of His property. And to the second that He has in every omission countless benefits, so wisdom is not violated. Moreover, necessity has no meaning except the inability to abandon, which contradicts free choice. So reflect.
Source pages p. 217

Commentary
This passage refutes the Mu'tazili claim that Allah is obligated to provide lutf (grace). First, it defines lutf as an act that aids obedience without compulsion, using prophecy as an example. The Mu'tazili argue that such acts are known by necessity to be beneficial. The refutation uses a counterexample: if a prophet were sent in every age and an infallible guide in every region, that would be even more of a grace, yet the Mu'tazilis do not require that. Thus the proof fails. The commentary then clarifies the Mu'tazili definition of rational obligation and offers two rebuttals: Allah as absolute owner is not subject to blame, and His omissions always involve wisdom and do not violate it; furthermore, necessity would imply inability to act, contradicting divine free will.

Key Terms
اللطف
الإلجاء
الوجوب العقلي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The refutation denies that Allah ever sends prophets or graces; it only denies the obligation.
Correction: The Ash'ari do not deny that Allah sends prophets out of grace; they deny that He is obligated to do so in a specific manner. The Mu'tazili proof is self-defeating because it would require more than they accept.

Checkpoint
What counterexample does the author use to refute the obligation of lutf?
A) The creation of evil in the world
B) The sending of prophets in every age and region
C) The rewarding of believers in the afterlife
D) The existence of free will

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari refutation of lutf shows that the Mu'tazili proof is self-defeating and fails to account for divine sovereignty and wisdom.

Refutation of the Mu'tazili Obligation of Reward
Refutes the Mu'tazili claim that reward is obligatory upon Allah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Mu'tazili argument for obligatory reward is rationally unsound and what alternative views on taklif are proposed.

Core Idea
Reward is not an obligation upon Allah; it is a favor that human acts cannot merit.

The Mu'tazilites argue that Allah must reward obedience because failure would be evil and taklif must have a beneficial purpose. This lesson examines their reasoning and presents the refutation, showing that human acts are incomparable to divine blessings, and that taklif can be purposeless or for the harm and benefit of different groups without obligation.

Guided Path
1
Read the Mu'tazili Argument
Identify the two premises: reward is due because withholding it would be evil, and taklif must have a purpose beneficial to the servant.
2
Understand the Refutation
Notice the analogy of the servant moving a finger to repay the king's favors, showing the disparity between human acts and divine blessings.
This analogy demonstrates that human obedience is too insignificant to warrant obligatory reward.
3
Explore Alternative Views on Taklif
See the three possibilities: taklif may be purposeless (possible), or for harming some and benefiting others, or a non-obligatory favor.
These alternatives undermine the Mu'tazili assumption that taklif must have a beneficial purpose that obligates reward.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني: الثواب على الطاعة لأنه مستحق للعبد على الله الطاعة فالإخلال به قبيح وهو ممتنع عليه تعالى وإذا كان تركه ممتنعاً كان الإتيان به واجباً ( ولأن التكليف إما لا لغرض وهو عبث وإنه لجد قبيح) خصوصاً بالنسبة إلى الحكيم تعالى ( وإما لغرض إما عائد إلى الله تعالى وهو منزه عنه أو إلى العبد إما في الدنيا وإنه مشقة بلا حظ وإما في الاخرة وهو إما إضراره هو باطل إجماعا ) وقبيح من الجواد الكريم ( وإما نفعه وهو المطلوب ) لأن إيصال ذلك النفع واجب لئلا يلزم نقض الغرض ( فيقال لهم : الطاعة ( التي كلف بها لا تكافئ النعم السابقة لكثرتها وعظمها وحقارة أفعال العبد وقلتها بالنسبة إليها وما ذلك إلا كمن يقابل نعمة الملك عليه مما لا يحصره بتحريك أنملته. فكيف يحكم العقل بإيجابه الثواب عليه) واستحقاقه إياه . وأما التكليف فنختار أنه لا لغرض ولا استحالة فيه كما عن قريب ( أو ) هو ( لضرر قوم ) كالكافرين ( ونفع (آخرين كالمؤمنين ( كما هو الواقع أو ليس ذلك على سبيل الوجوب ) بل هو تفضل على الأبرار وعدل بالنسبة إلى

Second: reward for obedience, because it is due to the servant from Allah for obedience. Failure to give it is evil, and that is impossible for Allah. If its omission is impossible, then its fulfillment is obligatory. And because the obligation (taklif) either has no purpose, which is futile and extremely evil—especially for the Wise—or it has a purpose: either returning to Allah, which is above Him, or to the servant: either in this world, which is toil without benefit, or in the afterlife, which is either harm, which is unanimously false and evil from the Generous and Noble, or benefit, which is what is sought. Because conveying that benefit is obligatory to avoid defeating the purpose. So it is said to them: The obedience that is commanded does not equal the preceding blessings due to their multitude and greatness, and the insignificance and smallness of the servant's acts relative to them. This is only like one who reciprocates the king's uncountable blessings by moving his little finger. So how does reason judge that reward is obligatory upon Him and that it is due? As for taklif, we choose that it has no purpose and there is no impossibility in that, as will come shortly. Or it is for harm to some (like disbelievers) and benefit to others (like believers), as is the reality. Or it is not by way of obligation, but rather it is a favor upon the righteous and justice with respect to...
His saying: (And it is toil without benefit). You may say: It is toil, but why is it not permissible that a worldly compensation be obtained through it, as Abu Hashim and his followers said regarding pain?
Source pages p. 217

Commentary
This passage refutes the Mu'tazili claim that reward for obedience is obligatory upon Allah. It presents the Mu'tazili argument: reward is a due right because withholding it would be evil, and taklif must have a beneficial purpose for the servant. The refutation points out that human obedience is utterly incomparable to divine blessings—like a trivial gesture against immense royal favors—so reason cannot obligate reward. Then alternative views on taklif are given: it may be purposeless (possible), or aimed at harming some and benefiting others, or a non-obligatory favor. A gloss clarifies that even if taklif involves toil, a worldly compensation could be possible, as argued by Abu Hashim.

Correction: The refutation shows that human acts cannot merit obligatory reward; reward is a favor (tafaḍḍul) from Allah, not a required payment.

God as the Absolute Judge: No Obligation Upon Him
States the Ash'ari position that God is the absolute judge with no obligation upon Him.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari doctrine that God is the absolute sovereign, with no obligation imposed upon Him by reason or any external standard.

Core Idea
God is the judge, not the judged.

In this opening passage, the author directly confronts the Mu'tazilite claim that reason can impose obligations upon God. He reasserts the Ash'ari principle that God is the ultimate authority, subject to no external moral criteria. This foundational premise underlies all subsequent discussions of divine actions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the two opposing positions
Read the Arabic passage once for overall meaning, then read the translation. Notice how the author contrasts the Ash'ari position with the Mu'tazilite one.
The author first refutes reason as a judge of good and evil.
2
Understand the Ash'ari argument
Focus on the key phrases: 'God is the Judge, He judges what He wills,' 'no obligation upon Him,' 'no deeming of evil.' Why does the author say there is no obligation upon Him and no obligation from Him?
This establishes God's absolute sovereignty.
3
Note the author's method
The author states that refuting the Mu'tazilite specifics is done even though refuting their principle would suffice. This shows a thorough approach to refutation.
The principle is that reason can impose obligations on God.
